The Philosophers' Three Arguments for Vision as Impression
Presents the philosophers' view that vision is merely an impression on the eye, with three examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three empirical examples philosophers use to argue that vision is merely a physical impression on the eye.

Core Idea
Vision is not perception; it is just the eye being physically impressed.

The philosophers propose a radical view: what we call 'seeing' is nothing but a physical change in the eye. To support this, they offer three vivid examples drawn from everyday experience. Before we examine the theological refutation, we must first grasp these arguments on their own terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: a general claim followed by three numbered aspects.
The passage uses the word 'وجوه' meaning 'aspects' or 'examples'.
2
Identify the three examples
List each example: afterimage of the sun, green garden aftereffect, overpowering of strong light. Note how each demonstrates an impression on the pupil.
Each example relies on a familiar visual phenomenon.
3
Understand the claim
The philosophers assert that vision is nothing but the pupil's impression, not an increase in disclosure. Consider why this matters for theological discussion about the nature of perception.
This view reduces vision to a physical process, denying any spiritual or cognitive element.
4
Prepare for refutation
Reflect on possible objections to each example. The next lesson will challenge these arguments from an Islamic theological perspective.
Look for weaknesses in equating afterimages with actual vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة هي (أي تلك المغايرة والزيادة) عائدة إلى تأثر الحدقة لا إلى زيادة الانكشاف هي الرؤية والإبصار. الوجوه: الأول: إن من نظر إلى الشمس بالاستقصاء ثم غمض فإنه يتخيل أن الشمس حاضرة عنده لا يتأتى له أن يدفعه (أي هذا التخيل) عن نفسه أصلا وما ذلك إلا لأن الحدقة تأثرت عن صورة الشمس وبقيت صورتها في الحدقة بعد أن زالت الرؤية. الثاني: إن من نظر بالاستقصاء إلى روضة خضراء زماناً طويلاً ثم حول عينيه إلى شيء أبيض فإنه يرى لونه ممتزجاً من البياض والخضرة فقد تحقق أن حدقته تأثرت عن الخضرة وبقى صورتها فيها بعد التحول. الثالث: أن الضوء القوي يقهر الباصرة وكذلك البياض الشديد يقهرها بحيث لو نظر الرائي بعد رؤيتهما إلى ضوء ضعيف أو بياض ضعيف لم يرهما فلولا تأثرها (أي تأثر الحاسة) منه (بل منهما) لما كان الأمر كذلك.
The philosophers said: It (i.e., that difference and addition) is due to the impression of the pupil, not to an increase in disclosure. That is vision and sight. The aspects: First: one who looks intently at the sun and then closes his eyes, he imagines that the sun is present with him, and he cannot repel this imagining from himself at all. That is only because the pupil was impressed by the image of the sun and its image remained in the pupil after vision had ceased. Second: one who looks intently at a green garden for a long time and then turns his eyes to something white, he sees its color mixed with white and green. Thus it is confirmed that his pupil was impressed by the green and its image remained in it after the turning. Third: strong light overpowers the sight, and similarly intense white overpowers it, such that if the seer after seeing them looks at a weak light or weak white, he does not see them. So if it were not for the impression (i.e., of the sense) from it (rather from both), it would not be so.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument that vision is nothing but an impression (ta'aththur) on the pupil, not an additional disclosure (inkishaf). They offer three examples (wujuh) to prove this. First: after staring at the sun and closing the eyes, the afterimage persists, showing the pupil retains the image. Second: after staring at a green garden, a white surface appears mixed with green, indicating the pupil's impression. Third: strong light or whiteness overpowers the sight, making weak light invisible, proving the pupil is affected. These examples aim to show that vision is merely a physical impression, not a separate perception. This sets up the refutation in the next lesson.

Key Terms
فلاسفة
تأثر
حدقة
إبصار
انكشاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the philosophers deny all vision, but they only reduce it to physiological impression, not a real perception.
Correction: The philosophers do not deny that we see; they reinterpret vision as a physical effect on the eye rather than a separate act of perception.

Checkpoint
What do the philosophers claim is the cause of vision according to the three examples?
A) An increase in disclosure (زيادة الانكشاف)
B) The impression of the pupil (تأثر الحدقة)
C) The presence of light
D) The will of the seer

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that vision is merely a physical impression on the pupil, not a separate act of perception, as shown by afterimages and overpowering effects.

What would it take for you to look beyond the physical impressions and see His signs in everything?

The Ash'ari Doctrine on Human Acts: Creation by God, Acquisition by Humans
The author states the Ash'ari doctrine and contrasts it with two other views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that human acts are created solely by God and acquired by humans, and how it differs from Mu'tazilite and dual-power views.

Core Idea
Humans acquire acts but do not create them; God alone is the creator.

This passage presents the core Ash'ari doctrine on human acts, clarifying a subtle but crucial distinction: God creates the act, while the human acquires it by being the locus of its occurrence. This position is then contrasted with two other views, helping you see how the Ash'ari school navigates between divine omnipotence and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the author first states the Ash'ari view, then mentions the Mu'tazilite view, and finally a third view.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three positions
Find and label each of the three positions: Ash'ari, Mu'tazilite, and the dual-power view. Pay attention to how each describes the role of God and the servant.
3
Understand 'acquisition' (kasb)
Focus on the definition of 'kasb': the act's concomitance to the servant's power and will without any causal effect. Contrast this with the idea that the servant creates his own act.
This is the key technical term in the Ash'ari position.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: why is it important that God alone creates? How does kasb preserve human responsibility without attributing creative power to humans?
This connects to broader theological debates on predestination and free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس في أفعاله تعالى وفيه مقاصد ] [ المقصد الأول : في أفعاله تعالى وأفعال العباد ] في أن أفعال العباد الاختيارية واقعة بقدرة الله سبحانه وتعالى وحدها) ليس لقدرتهم تأثير فيها بل الله سبحانه أجرى عادته بأن يوجد في العبد قدرة واختياراً فإذا لم يكن هناك مانع أوجد فيه فعله المقدور مقارناً لهما، فيكون فعل العبد مخلوقاً لله إبداعاً وإحداثاً ومكسوباً للعبد ، والمراد بكسبه إياه مقارنته لقدرته وإرادته من غير أن يكون هناك منه تأثير أو مدخل في وجوده سوی کونه محلاً له، وهذا مذهب الشيخ الحسن الأشعري ) وقالت المعتزلة : ( أي أكثرهم هي ( واقعة بقدرة العبد وحدها ) على سبيل الاستقلال بلا إيجاب بل باختيار ) وقالت طائفة ) هي واقعة (بالقدرتين)
The sixth observation on His actions, and in it aims. The first aim: on His actions and the actions of servants. [It is] that the voluntary acts of servants are brought about by the power of God alone. Their power has no effect in them, but God has established His custom that He creates in the servant power and choice; then if there is no impediment, He creates in him his destined act concomitant to them. So the servant's act is created by God as origination and invention, and acquired (maksūb) by the servant. What is meant by his acquisition of it is its concomitance to his power and will without there being from him any effect or entrance into its existence except his being a locus for it. This is the doctrine of Shaykh al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. The Mu'tazila (i.e., most of them) said: it is brought about by the servant's power alone, independently, without necessitation but with choice. And a group said: it is brought about by both powers.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage classifies three positions on human actions. The Ash'ari position: God alone creates the act; the human power has no causal effect; the act is 'acquired' by the human because it occurs concomitantly with his power and will. The human is merely the locus. The Mu'tazilite position: the servant independently creates his own act by his power with choice. A third group says both powers are involved.

Key Terms

اختيار
كسب
مخلوق
إبداع
إحداث
محض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'acquisition' (kasb) means humans create their own acts.
Correction: Kasb is mere concomitance; God creates the act, and the human only 'acquires' it by being the locus of its occurrence with no causal role.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the role of human power in the Ash'ari view?
A) It creates the act independently
B) It has no effect; God alone creates
C) It cooperates with God's power
D) It is nonexistent

Key Takeaway
In Ash'ari theology, human acts are created solely by God and acquired by humans as concomitant to their power and will, preserving divine omnipotence and human accountability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that your voluntary acts are created solely by God, and your power has 'no effect' on their existence. When you recall your greatest achievement, can you honestly see yourself as merely a 'locus' in which God created that success? What happens to your pride when you internalize this? Take a moment to rewrite that achievement in your mind as entirely from God, and thank Him for it.
?
If your choices are created by God but you are held accountable for them, how does that change the way you approach a repeated sin you struggle with? Are you tempted to blame God, or does the doctrine of kasb strengthen your sense of personal responsibility? Resolve tonight to repent sincerely, acknowledging both God's sole creation and your own acquisition.

Divine Creation Includes Animals and the Prophet's Speech
The author extends divine creation to animals and the Prophet's speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Ash'ari doctrine of divine creation of actions applies not only to humans but to all voluntary acts, including those of animals and the Prophet's verbal speech.

Core Idea
All voluntary acts, from animals to prophets, are created by Allah.

The discussion of divine creation of actions is not limited to human beings. The author clarifies that the same principle applies to the voluntary acts of all creatures, including animals and even the Prophet's speech. This lesson unpacks that extension and addresses a potential exception regarding the Prophet's speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Note the author's style and the scope of the claim.
The passage is a continuation of the discussion on voluntary acts.
2
Identify the two extensions
The passage explicitly mentions two cases beyond general human acts: animals (الحيوانات العجم) and the Prophet's verbal speech (الكلام اللفظي القائم بالنبي). Try to find these phrases.
3
Understand the author's reasoning
The author states there is no dispute that these are created by Allah. For the Prophet's speech, he adds a condition 'on the assumption of its temporal origination' and mentions the Mu'tazila's possible stance.
This shows the author is addressing potential objections.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the author did not elaborate on animals' acts. The passage says because there is little benefit in disputing that. This highlights the focus on theological controversies that matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( في أن أفعال العباد الاختيارية إلخ لا خلاف في أن أفعال الغير الاختيارية للعباد مخلوقة له تعالى كما هو المشهور ولا في أن الكلام اللفظي القائم بالنبي عليه الصلاة والسلام على تقدير حدوثه مخلوقة له تعالى أيضا، أما عندنا فظاهر وأما عند المعتزلة فإما بنفي اختياريته أو باستثنائه عن الكلية كما مر مع ما فيه في بحث الكلام ثم إن البحث يعم الحيوانات العجم صرح به في أبكار الأفكار والمنقذ ، ولما لم يكن للنزاع في أفعالها الاختيارية كثير فائدة لم يتعرض لها وقال في أن أفعال العباد إلخ .
His statement: 'On the voluntary acts of servants, etc.' There is no dispute that the voluntary acts of others [by God's creatures?] are created by Him, as is well-known, nor that the verbal speech subsisting in the Prophet (peace be upon him) — on the assumption of its temporal origination — is also created by Him. As for us, it is apparent; as for the Mu'tazila, either by denying its voluntary nature or by exempting it from the generality, as previously mentioned with its issues in the discussion of speech. Moreover, the investigation includes non-rational animals, as stated in Abkar al-Afkar and al-Munqidh. Since the dispute over their voluntary acts does not have much benefit, he did not address it. And he said: 'On the acts of servants, etc.'
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage clarifies that the statement about human actions applies more broadly. The voluntary acts of any being (including animals) are created by God. Also, the created speech of the Prophet (kalām lafẓī) is created by God, though the Mu'tazila may treat it differently. The note mentions sources.

Key Terms

حدوث
حيوانات عجم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: That the Ash'ari doctrine of divine creation of actions only applies to human beings.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the discussion includes non-rational animals and the Prophet's verbal speech, indicating that all voluntary acts of any creature are created by Allah.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the status of the verbal speech of the Prophet?

B) It is created by God on the assumption of its temporality
C) It is created by the Prophet himself

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position that all voluntary acts are created by Allah includes animals and even the Prophet's speech, with no real exception.

The Conditional 'If There Is No Impediment' Explained
Objection and answer about conditional clause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the conditional 'if there is no impediment' is included in the Ash'ari statement and how it relates to divine custom.

Core Idea
The conditional clause reflects divine custom, not a separate existence of power.

This passage clarifies a potential misunderstanding about the Ash'ari position on power and action. The author addresses an objection that the conditional seems redundant, explaining that it refers to God's customary way of acting, not to a real possibility that power exists without the act. Let's read the source.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the objection and response.
Pay attention to the distinction between two interpretations.
2
Identify the objection
The objection claims the conditional is redundant because according to Sunni principles, power is necessarily with the act. Find this in the passage.
3
Understand the response
The response distinguishes two contexts: the conditional is not about the creation of power but about the divine custom. Understand the meaning of 'jaran 'adati'.
The conditional is precautionary redundancy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فإذا لم يكن هناك مانع إلخ قلت : هذه الشرطية مستدركة فإن الكلام مسوق على قواعد أهل السنة والقدرة عندهم مع الفعل البتة فبعد إيجاد القدرة في العبد لا مجال للمانع ، قلت : ليس قوله فإذا لم يكن هناك مانع إلخ متفرعا على إيجاد القدرة بل على جريان عادته تعالى بأن يوجد فيه ذلك، والمعنى لما كان عادته تعالى جارية بذلك فإذا لم يكن هناك مانع لصدور الفعل عن العبد أوجد الله تعالى فيه فعلاً مقارناً لإيجاد القدرة فعلى هذا الاستدراك فتدبر.
His statement: 'then if there is no impediment, etc.' I said: This conditional is redundant, because the discourse proceeds on the principles of the People of the Sunna, and power with them is necessarily with the act. So after the creation of power in the servant, there is no room for an impediment. [Another] said: His statement 'if there is no impediment, etc.' is not branched from the creation of power, but from the continuation of His custom that He creates that in him. The meaning: since His custom continues accordingly, then if there is no impediment to the act issuing from the servant, God creates in him an act concomitant to the creation of power. So this is a [precautionary] redundancy. Reflect.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection: Why include the conditional 'if there is no impediment' if, according to Ash'ari principles, power is always with the act? The answer distinguishes two senses: the conditional refers to the divine custom, not to the separate existence of power. It is just a clarification.

Key Terms
شرطية
قدرة
مانع
عادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the conditional means that power can exist without the act
Correction: It actually refers to divine custom, not a real condition on power.

Checkpoint
Why is the conditional 'if there is no impediment' included according to the explanation?
A) Because power can exist without the act
B) Because it refers to the divine custom of creating the act only when no impediment exists in the locus
C) Because the act is independent of power
D) Because impediment always prevents

Key Takeaway
The conditional 'if there is no impediment' is a precautionary reference to divine custom, not a real condition on power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What impediment in your life have you been using as an excuse to delay obedience, and how does knowing that God's custom is to empower you when there is no obstacle move you to act today?
?
The conditional clause is a precaution about divine custom, not a limitation. Is there a promise of God you have treated as conditional on your perfection rather than as His consistent custom? How will you realign your trust in His ways?
?
If the conditional reflects God's usual pattern, what 'impediment' do you need to remove from your heart to let His power produce the act He desires from you? What is one step you will take to clear that impediment?

Proof of Human Power Without Causal Effect
Objection and answer on knowing human power without causal effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari school justifies knowledge of human power (qudra) through immediate necessity (ḍarūra) even though it has no causal effect.

Core Idea
Immediate awareness, not causal effect, proves human power.

This passage addresses a classic objection to the Ash'ari view of human power. If our power has no causal effect, how can we even know it exists? The answer lies in the concept of immediate necessity (ḍarūra) — we simply feel our power when we act voluntarily. Let's read the text and see how the author defends this.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how the objector argues that power is known by its effect, and since Ash'aris deny any effect, knowledge seems impossible.
The objector also mentions the Jabriyya who deny power altogether.
2
Understand the answer
The answer invokes immediate necessity (ḍarūra). It claims that we directly witness power conjoined with will in voluntary acts, even without seeing any separate effect. Reflect on what 'immediate necessity' means in this context.
This is a distinct epistemological claim, not based on empirical proof.
3
Reflect on the role of necessity
Consider how this argument defends the Ash'ari position. Why is 'conscience' (al-wijdān) insufficient? How does necessity differ from ordinary perception?
Necessity is a self-evident, undeniable awareness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وهذا مذهب الشيخ قيل عليه : ثبوت القدر إنما يعلم بأثرها من الفعل ولما لم يكن لقدرة العبد أثر عند الأشعري فمن أين يعلم ثبوتها ويرد على الجبرية النافين لها مطلقاً، والوجدان إنما يشهد بثبوت الشعور والإرادة فينا وليسا بأثرين للقدرة والجواب أن الضرورة تشهد بوجود القدرة منضماً إلى الإرادة في الأفعال الاختيارية دون غيرها وإن لم تشهد بتأثيرها أصلاً، كما سيصرح به .
His statement: 'This is the doctrine of the Shaykh.' It was objected to him: The establishment of power is only known by its effect from the act, and since the servant's power has no effect according to al-Ash'ari, from where is its establishment known? And this objection applies to the Jabriyya who deny it absolutely. Conscience only witnesses the establishment of consciousness and will in us, and these are not two effects of power. The answer: that necessity (ḍarūra) witnesses the existence of power conjoined with will in voluntary acts, unlike other acts, even if it does not witness its effect at all, as will be clarified.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This is an objection and answer. The objector says that power is known by its effect, but Ash'aris deny any effect of human power, so how can they know it exists? The answer: immediate awareness (ḍarūra) tells us we have power when we act voluntarily, even without perceiving an effect separate from the act.

Key Terms
قدرة
أثر
ضرورة
وجدان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power can only be known if it produces a separate observable effect.
Correction: Ash'aris hold that power is known by immediate necessity (ḍarūra) through the feeling of volitional action, even if no separate effect is perceived.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, how is the existence of human power known?
A) By its effect on the act
B) By revelation
C) By immediate necessity (ḍarūra) witnessing it conjoined with will in voluntary acts
D) By rational deduction

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari doctrine affirms knowledge of human power through immediate necessity, not through causal effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accomplish something, do you instinctively feel you caused it? How does the teaching that your power has no causal effect but is still real change your self-perception?
?
Think of a time you acted righteously despite doubt about your ability. Did you rely on an inner sense of power or on external evidence? How can you cultivate reliance on immediate awareness (ḍarūra) over visible results?
?
If your power has no effect, why do you still feel responsible for your actions? How does this lesson's distinction between power and effect shape your accountability before Allah?

Clarifying the Conjunction of Two Influences
Defends al-Ustadh from objection about conjunction of influences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ustadh's view on the conjunction of two influences can be interpreted to avoid the objection of two independent causes acting on one effect.

Core Idea
Human power is not independent; it becomes effective through God's power.

The first gloss addresses a major objection to al-Ustadh's position: the claim that he permits two influences (God's and human's) to act on a single effect. The commentator offers a charitable interpretation that preserves the unity of causal influence while maintaining human responsibility. Let's examine how this defense works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the first part of the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the key phrase 'lā yuridu 'alayhi luzūm ijtima' al-mu'aththirayn'.
Notice that the commentator is interpreting, not stating al-Ustadh's own words.
2
Identify the interpretation
Find the two proposed interpretations: (a) each power is part of the total influence, or (b) human power becomes independent through divine assistance.
These avoid the problem of two independent influences meeting on one effect.
3
Note the caveat
The commentator notes that the well-known view of al-Ustadh is different. Reflect on why this matters for the overall argument.
This shows the commentator is not endorsing the interpretation as definitive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وجوز اجتماع المؤثرين على أثر واحد قيل : لعل مراد الأستاذ أن قدرة العبد غير مستقلة بالتأثير وإذا انضمت إليه قدرة الله تعالى صار المجموع مؤثراً في الفعل على أن كلاً منهما جزء المؤثر أو صارت قدرة العبد مستقلة بتوسط هذه الإعانة وعلى هذا لا يرد عليه لزوم اجتماع المؤثرين على أثر واحد لكن المشهور من مذهبه ما ذكره الشارح.

قوله : ( أي بكونه طاعة ومعصية يرد عليه أن هذه الصفة أمر اعتباري يلزم فعل العبد من موافقته لما أمر الله تعالى به أو مخالفته له فلا وجه لجعله أثراً للقدرة كيف ولو قيل : بوجودية الصفة المذكورة واستنادها إلى قدرة العبد لكان العبد موجوداً لبعض الأشياء وهو مذهب بعض الاعتزال ويحتمل أن يقال كون الفعل طاعة أو معصية لما عرضه بالنسبة إلى محله نسبة إلى قدرة العبد وإن لم ينشأ منها .
His statement: 'And he permitted the conjunction of two influences on one effect.' It is said: Perhaps al-Ustadh meant that the servant's power is not independent in influence, and when God's power is joined to it, the collective becomes influential in the act, either as each being a part of the influence or the servant's power becoming independent through this assistance. On this, the necessary consequence of the conjunction of two influences on one effect does not apply to him. But what is well-known of his doctrine is what the commentator mentioned.

His statement: 'i.e., being obedience or disobedience.' It is objected that this quality is a relative matter that follows from the servant's act being in agreement with what God commanded or in opposition to it, so there is no basis for making it an effect of power. Moreover, if one were to say that the mentioned quality is existent and is attributed to the servant's power, then the servant would be an originator of some things, which is the doctrine of certain Mu'tazila. It is possible to say that the act's being obedience or disobedience, due to its relation to its subject, is attributed to the servant's power even if it does not arise from it.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage contains two separate glosses on statements made earlier.

The first gloss addresses the claim that al-Ustadh permitted the conjunction of two influences on one effect. The commentator offers an interpretation: al-Ustadh may have meant that the servant's power is not independent in its influence; it becomes effective only when joined by God's power. The combined force then becomes the influential factor, either as each being a part of the whole influence, or the servant's power becoming independent through that assistance. Under this interpretation, the objection that two independent influences cannot meet on one effect does not apply. However, the commentator notes that the well-known view of al-Ustadh is what the main commentator (the author of Sharh al-Mawaqif) mentioned earlier.

The second gloss discusses the statement that the servant's power attaches to the quality of the act (being obedience or disobedience). The objection here is that such a quality is a relative matter (amr i'tibari) that arises from the act's conformity or non-conformity to God's command, so it is not a real effect of power. If one were to claim that this quality is an existing attribute and is caused by the servant's power, that would make the servant a creator of something, which is the view of some Mu'tazila. The commentator then proposes a compromise: it is possible to say that the act is called obedience or disobedience due to its relation to its subject (the person performing it) and this relation is attributed to the servant's power, even if the quality does not originate from the power itself.

Key Terms
اجتماع المؤثرين
جزء المؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first interpretation, how does human power become effective?
A) It is completely independent
B) It is a part of the total influence with God's power
C) It is fully dependent on God's power alone
D) It becomes effective only when God's power is absent

Key Takeaway
Al-Ustadh's view on conjunction of influences can be interpreted in two ways that avoid the objection of two independent causes, though his true position is what the commentator stated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you succeed in a difficult task, do you secretly feel that your own ability was the decisive factor, or do you truly recognize that God's power was the source? What would change in your daily attitude if you genuinely internalized that your power is never independent?
?
Consider a time you attributed an achievement to your own effort. Can you now rephrase that experience in light of this lesson—admitting that your power was only effective through God's assistance? Write down one sentence that captures that dependence.
?
Al-Ustadh's view suggests that human power becomes effective 'through assistance.' Do you live as if you need God's assistance for every action, or do you unconsciously rely on your own strength? What specific action today can you perform with conscious dependence on God?

The Quality of Acts as Relational Attribute
Explains why obedience/disobedience are not direct effects of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why obedience and disobedience are considered relational qualities (amr i'tibari) rather than real effects of human power, and how they can still be attributed to human action.

Core Idea
Obedience/disobedience are relational, not caused by power.

The second gloss tackles another tricky point: how can human power be said to 'attach' to the quality of an act (being obedience or disobedience)? The objection is that these are not real, existing qualities but relative labels. The commentator explains why they cannot be direct effects of power and offers a nuanced position that avoids Mu'tazilite implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the second part of the Arabic and its translation. Focus on the phrase 'anna hādhihi al-sifah amr i'tibārī'.
The term i'tibārī means relational or mental consideration.
2
Understand the objection
The objection says obedience/disobedience are not real attributes produced by power; they are merely relative to God's command.
This challenges the idea that human power has any role in making an act good or bad.
3
Consider the Mu'tazilite view
The text warns that if these qualities were real and caused by human power, that would be the Mu'tazilite position.
This shows sectarian boundaries.
4
Learn the alternative
The commentator suggests that these qualities can be attributed to the servant's power by way of relation, not origination.
This preserves human responsibility without making the servant a creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وجوز اجتماع المؤثرين على أثر واحد قيل : لعل مراد الأستاذ أن قدرة العبد غير مستقلة بالتأثير وإذا انضمت إليه قدرة الله تعالى صار المجموع مؤثراً في الفعل على أن كلاً منهما جزء المؤثر أو صارت قدرة العبد مستقلة بتوسط هذه الإعانة وعلى هذا لا يرد عليه لزوم اجتماع المؤثرين على أثر واحد لكن المشهور من مذهبه ما ذكره الشارح.

On this, the necessary consequence of the conjunction of two influences on one effect does not apply to him. But what is well-known of his doctrine is what the commentator mentioned.

However, the commentator notes that the well-known view of al-Ustadh is what the main commentator (the author of Sharh al-Mawaqif) mentioned earlier.

Key Terms
الصفة الاعتبارية
الطاعة
المعصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That obedience and disobedience are real, created qualities caused by human power.
Correction: They are relational attributes (amr i'tibari) that follow from conformity or opposition to God's command, not directly caused by human power.

Checkpoint
Why is it problematic to say that obedience/disobedience are real effects of human power?
A) Because it would imply that God is the source of evil
B) Because it would make humans creators of some things, which is Mu'tazilite
C) Because obedience and disobedience are not real at all
D) Because human power cannot produce anything

Key Takeaway
Obedience and disobedience are relational qualities attributed to human power by relation, not by causation, avoiding both Mu'tazilite extremes and complete denial of human role.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look back at your own acts of obedience, do you find yourself taking pride as if you created that goodness yourself? How does this lesson's distinction between relational attribution and causal power force you to redirect that pride toward the One who commands? This week, when you perform an act of obedience, consciously say to yourself: 'This label of obedience is not from my power but from Allah’s command.' What changes in your heart when you do that?
?
If obedience and disobedience are merely relational labels based on conformity to God’s command, then what is the true root of your accountability? Is it your power, or your relationship to the command? Think of a specific sin you committed recently. Did you excuse yourself by saying 'I couldn't help it' or 'That's just how I am'? How does this lesson demolish that excuse and place the weight back on your response to Allah’s command? Will you still use that excuse tonight?
?
The passage offers a middle way: the quality is attributed to your power relationally, not causally. Does this lighten or deepen your sense of responsibility? Reflect on a moment when you felt your sin was purely your own doing, and you despaired. Now see it through this lesson: you are connected to the sin, but its existence is not from you. How does that balance of accountability and humility change the way you approach repentance? What specific step will you take tomorrow to acknowledge both your connection to the act and your complete dependence on Allah?

The Rule (al-Dabit) for Power Relations and the Views of Leading Scholars
Presents the rule (al-dabit) for power relations and positions of various schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rule (al-dabit) that classifies all possible relations between divine and human power in causing action, and be able to identify the positions of the philosophers, Imam al-Haramayn, al-Ustadh, al-Najjar, and al-Qadi.

Core Idea
All possible relations of power are captured by a single rule: either one power works alone, or both work together—with or without unity.

The complex debate about whether human actions are created solely by God or also involve human power is reduced by al-Sharif al-Jurjani to a systematic rule (al-dabit). This rule enumerates every logical possibility for how divine and human power might relate in producing an effect. By working through the rule, we can see precisely where each school stands.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial statement
Read the opening sentence stating the view of the philosophers and Imam al-Haramayn: the act occurs by necessity through a created power.
2
Identify the rule (al-dabit)
Recognize the rule that the influence is either God's power alone, human power alone, or both together.
The rule is introduced with 'والضابط' in the passage.
3
Explore the subcases of both powers together
Note the two subcases: with unity of the two objects of attachment (the same aspect) or without unity.
4
Understand the without-unity subcases
See that without unity, either one power is attached to the other (the view of Imam al-Haramayn and philosophers) or neither is attached (the view of al-Qadi).
5
Consider the hypothetical objection
Reflect on the objection about reversing the roles and why it is rejected.
The passage ends by dismissing the reverse view as unheard of.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العبد وتأثيره وقالت الحكماء وإمام الحرمين) هي واقعة على سبيل الوجوب وامتناع ( التخلف ) بقدرة يخلقها الله تعالى في العبد إذا قارنت حصول الشرائط وارتفاع الموانع ( والضابط ) في هذا المقام ( أن المؤثر إما قدرة الله أو قدرة العبد ) على الانفراد كمذهبي الشيخ وجمهور المعتزلة ( أو هما معاً، ذلك إما ( مع اتحاد المتعلقين) كمذهب الأستاذ منا والنجار من المعتزلة ( أو دونه ( أي دون الاتحاد ( وحينئذ فإما مع كون إحداهما ) أي إحدى القدرتين ( متعلقة للأخرى، و) لا شبهة في أنه (ليس قدرة الله متعلقة لقدرة العبد ) إذ يستحيل تأثير الحادث في القديم فتعين العكس أن تكون قدرة العبد صادرة عن قدرة الله تعالى وموجبة للفعل، وهو قول الإمام والفلاسفة ( وإما بدون ذلك ) أي بدون أن تكون إحداهما متعلقة للأخرى وهو مذهب القاضي لأن المفروض عدم اتحاد المتعلقتين، فإن قيل : جاز أن يكون عكس مذهبه أن أصل الفعل بقدرة العبد وصفته بقدرة الله قلنا : لم يقل به أحد والمقصود
The servant and his influence. The philosophers and Imam al-Haramayn said: It occurs by way of necessity and impossibility of non-occurrence, by a power that God creates in the servant when the conditions are present and obstacles are removed. The rule in this regard is that the influence is either God's power or the servant's power alone, as in the two schools of al-Shaykh and the majority of the Mu'tazila, or both together. That is either with the unity of the two objects of attachment, as in the school of al-Ustadh from us and al-Najjar from the Mu'tazila, or without unity. Then it is either with one of them (i.e., the two powers) being attached to the other, and there is no doubt that God's power is not attached to the servant's power, since it is impossible for the contingent to influence the eternal; so the opposite is determined: that the servant's power proceeds from God's power and necessitates the act, which is the view of Imam al-Haramayn and the philosophers. Or without that, i.e., without one of them being attached to the other, which is the school of al-Qadi, because the assumption is the non-unity of the two objects of attachment. If it is said: it is possible that the reverse of his school is that the essence of the act is by the servant's power and its quality by God's power, we say: no one has said that. And the intended [meaning].
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage first states the view of the philosophers (al-hukama') and Imam al-Haramayn: the action occurs necessarily and cannot fail to occur, by a power that God creates in the servant when conditions are met and obstacles are removed. Then it presents a 'rule' (al-dabit) that classifies all possible relationships between the two powers (divine and human) in influencing an action. The rule distinguishes three scenarios:

1. Either God's power alone or the servant's power alone is influential — this is the view of al-Shaykh (Abu'l-Hasan al-Ash'ari?) and the majority of Mu'tazila.
2. Or both powers together are influential. This has two subcases:
   a) With unity of the two objects of attachment (the same aspect of the act) — the view of al-Ustadh from the Ash'arites and al-Najjar from the Mu'tazila.
   b) Without unity of the two objects of attachment.
      Within this, there are two further possibilities:
         i) One power is attached to (i.e., causes) the other. Since God's power cannot be caused by the servant's power, the only possibility is that the servant's power is produced by God's power and necessitates the act — this is the view of Imam al-Haramayn and the philosophers.
         ii) Neither power is attached to the other — this is the view of al-Qadi, because there is assumed to be no unity of the two objects of attachment (each power attaches to a different aspect). The passage then addresses a hypothetical objection: someone might propose the reverse of al-Qadi's view, that the essence of the act is from the servant's power and its quality from God's power; but the commentator responds that no one has held such a view.

Key Terms
الوجوب
امتناع التخلف
الضابط
المؤثر
اتحاد المتعلقين
القدرة القديمة
القدرة الحادثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It might be thought that the servant's power could cause God's power or that a reverse scenario (essence from servant, quality from God) is plausible.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that it is impossible for the contingent (the servant's power) to influence the eternal (God), so only God's power can cause the servant's power. The reverse scenario is declared as never having been held by anyone.

Checkpoint
According to the rule (al-dabit), which case represents the view of Imam al-Haramayn and the philosophers?
A) Both powers together with unity of objects of attachment
B) Both powers together without unity, with one power attached to the other
C) Both powers together without unity, with no attachment between them
D) Only God's power alone

Key Takeaway
The rule (al-dabit) shows that all possible relations of divine and human power fall into three main categories: one alone, both together with unity, or both together without unity—which further splits into one power causing the other or no causal relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you attribute it to God's decree or to your own weakness? The rule shows that your action stems from a power created by God yet necessitates the act only when conditions are met—so your sin is truly your choice under God's permission. How does this accountability press on your soul today?
?
Do you live as though you are the sole author of your actions, or do you acknowledge that every aspect—the essence and quality—is sustained by God? The passage dismisses a position where you control the essence and God only adds quality. What would change in your prayer, your work, and your relationships if you truly internalised that God's power is the ultimate origin of every detail of your act?
?
The rule eliminates the possibility that your will could compel God's power. Have you ever, in your heart, thought that your striving could force God's hand—that your exertion obligates His response? How does this logical impossibility challenge the way you approach your du'as and efforts?

Critique of the Classification of Influences
Critique of the rule's classification of influences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the rule of classifying influences into two types fails to distinguish the Mu'tazila from the philosophers.

Core Idea
The rule's classification collapses because both schools accept a chain of influences.

Sanusi's rule aimed to neatly separate the philosophers and Mu'tazila by identifying whether the proximate influence is God's power, human power, or both. But this gloss reveals that the distinction is not so clean. Let's read the critique carefully and see why the rule does not hold.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Get a sense of the argument before diving into analysis.
2
Identify the disjunction problem
The gloss points out that the disjunction (either God's power alone, or human power alone, or both) can be interpreted in two ways: proximate influence or absolute influence. Understand why each interpretation fails to capture the schools' views.
3
Analyze the hypothetical condition
The gloss says the rule would work only if the Mu'tazila held a specific view (power proceeding from the servant by necessity). Notice that this does not match their actual doctrine.
This shows the author's critical method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وقالت الحكماء وإمام الحرمين إلخ ) قال شارح المقاصد : هذا النقل من الإمام وإن الكتب إلا أنه خلاف ما صرح به في الإرشاد وغيره . قوله : ( أن المؤثر إما قدرة الله تعالى إلخ) فيه بحث لأن الترديد إما في المؤثر القريب في الفعل فلا يستقيم في قول الفلاسفة والإمام، أما في مطلق المؤثر فلا يتم الانفراد في قول المعتزلة لأن القدرة الحادثة أثر القدرة القديمة، وإن اختير الأول ووجه بأن مجموع القدرتين مؤثر قريب الفعل، وإن كانت القديمة وحدها مؤثرة بعيدة قلنا بعد تسليم صحته، فكذلك عند المعتزلة وبالجملة كما لم يظهر فرق بين مذهبي الفلاسفة والمعتزلة مما ذكره في التفصيل السابق لم يظهر من هذا الضابط أيضاً، نعم لو ذهبت المعتزلة إلى أن قدرة العبد صادرة عنه أيضاً لكن لا بطريق الاختيار لا يلزم التسلسل بل بطريق الإيجاب لظهر الفرق بين مذهبهم ومذهب الفلاسفة وصح ما ذكره في الضابط لكن هذا خلاف ما اشتهر منهم والله أعلم .

قوله : ( وهو مذهب القاضي قيل : هذا مشكل لأن المفروض عدم كون إحدى القدرتين متعلقة بالأخرى، أي أثر لها ونسبة هذا إلى القاضي بعيد فلا يبعد أن يقال : مقصود المصنف ضبط الاحتمالات العقلية، ومذهب القاضي مندرج فيما سبق والأخير مما لم يذهب إليه أحد إلا أن في الانحصار فيما ذكر نظراً. والجواب: يحتاج إلى أدنى عناية يشهد بها السياق، وهو أن يقال : المراد من قوله : فأما مع كون إحداهما متعلقة للأخرى أن يكون إحداهما فقط متعلقة بالأخرى أي لا يكون أصل الفعل ولا وصفه متعلقاً بها فالنفي الذي هو مذهب القاضي راجع إلى قيد فقط لأن القدرة القديمة ،عنده كما تتعلق بقدرة العبد على أصل الفعل تتعلق بوصف الفعل أيضاً ابتداء .
His statement: 'And the philosophers and Imam al-Haramayn, etc.' The commentator of al-Maqasid said: This transmission from Imam al-Haramayn in [some] books is contrary to what he stated in al-Irshad and elsewhere. His statement: 'The influence is either God's power, etc.' There is discussion in it because the disjunction is either with respect to the proximate influence in the act, in which case it is not correct for the statement of the philosophers and Imam; or with respect to the absolute influence, in which case the exclusivity is not complete for the statement of the Mu'tazila, because the contingent power is an effect of the eternal power. If the first is chosen and it is argued that the sum of the two powers is the proximate influence of the act, even though the eternal power alone is a remote influence, then we say, after accepting its validity, [that it is] likewise for the Mu'tazila. In short, just as no difference appeared between the schools of the philosophers and the Mu'tazila from what was mentioned in the previous detail, so also no difference appears from this rule. Yes, if the Mu'tazila had held that the servant's power also proceeds from him, but not by way of choice, so that no infinite regress follows, rather by way of necessity, then the difference between their school and the philosophers' school would appear and what was mentioned in the rule would be correct, but this opposes what is well-known from them. And God knows best.

His statement: 'And it is the school of al-Qadi.' It is said: This is problematic because the assumption is that one of the two powers is not attached to the other, i.e., is not an effect of it, and attributing this to al-Qadi is far-fetched. So it is not far-fetched to say that the author's intention is to enumerate the rational possibilities, and al-Qadi's school is included in what preceded, and the last [case] is one that no one has adopted. However, in the restriction to what was mentioned there is consideration. The answer requires minimal attention, as the context attests. It is that the intended meaning of his statement 'then if it is with one of them attached to the other' is that only one of them is attached to the other, meaning that neither the essence of the act nor its quality is attached to it. So the negation that is the school of al-Qadi refers only to the restriction, because the eternal power, according to him, just as it attaches to the servant's power regarding the essence of the act, also attaches to the quality of the act directly.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage contains two glosses that critique and clarify the rule (al-dabit) presented earlier.

The first gloss addresses the statement attributing the view of the philosophers and Imam al-Haramayn. It notes a discrepancy: the commentator of al-Maqasid says that this transmission from Imam al-Haramayn is contrary to what he explicitly stated in al-Irshad and other works. Then the gloss examines the classification of influence (al-mu'aththir). It raises a problem: the disjunction (either God's power or human power alone, or both together) can be interpreted in two ways. If it refers to the proximate influence in the act, then the philosophers' and Imam's view (that the act is by a power created by God) does not fit neatly. If it refers to absolute influence (including remote), then the Mu'tazila view that human power alone is influential is not correct, because the contingent power is itself an effect of the eternal power. Even if one chooses the first interpretation and argues that the sum of both powers is the proximate influence while the eternal power alone is remote, then the same logic applies to the Mu'tazila, eliminating the difference. The gloss concludes that the rule does not actually distinguish the schools; only if the Mu'tazila held that the servant's power proceeds from him by way of necessity (rather than choice) would the difference appear, but this contradicts their well-known position.

The second gloss addresses the placement of al-Qadi's view in the classification. It notes a problem: al-Qadi supposedly holds that neither power is attached to the other (no causality between them), but this is far-fetched. The answer (al-jawab) is that the author's intent was to list all rational possibilities, and al-Qadi's view can be understood as belonging to an earlier case (with attachment) but with a nuance: the negation of attachment is only partial — specifically, the eternal power attaches both to the essence of the act and to its quality directly, so there is no need for the human power to be attached to the divine power in a causal chain.

Key Terms
المؤثر القريب
المؤثر البعيد
الفرق بين الفلاسفة والمعتزلة
الإيجاب
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The rule of classifying influences (proximate vs. absolute) successfully distinguishes the Mu'tazila from the philosophers and Imam al-Haramayn.
Correction: The gloss shows that the rule fails to distinguish because both schools ultimately involve a chain of influences: the philosophers' view of created power and the Mu'tazila's view of contingent power both imply a remote eternal influence.

Checkpoint
What does the gloss identify as the main problem with the rule's disjunction of influences?
A) The disjunction is incomplete because it leaves out divine decree
B) The disjunction can be interpreted in two ways, and neither successfully distinguishes the schools
C) The disjunction contradicts the Quran
D) The disjunction only applies to the philosophers

Key Takeaway
The rule of influences, as presented, does not effectively differentiate the Mu'tazila from the philosophers; a hypothetical modification would work, but it does not reflect the Mu'tazila's actual doctrine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This critique shows that a seemingly clear classification can collapse under scrutiny. In your own life, are there theological assumptions you hold that you have not critically examined? Will you commit to questioning one such assumption today?
?
The gloss highlights the importance of precise definitions. When you speak about God's power and your own responsibility, are you careful to be accurate, or do you rely on vague phrases? What will you change to ensure your words reflect truth?
?
The hypothetical scenario reveals that a modified view would make the rule work, but it is rejected because it is not the actual doctrine. Are you ever tempted to adjust your beliefs to fit a neat system rather than accepting the revealed complexity? How will you guard against this?

Defending al-Qadi's Inclusion in the Rational Possibilities
Defense of al-Qadi's inclusion in the enumeration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Qadi's view is plausibly included in the classification of the two powers and why the objection of far-fetchedness is resolved.

Core Idea
Al-Qadi's view is not a separate case but a nuance within an existing case.

The previous classification listed possibilities regarding two powers (God's and the servant's) and whether one is attached to the other. Some objected that al-Qadi's position does not fit, but this gloss defends the inclusion by reinterpreting the condition. Let's read how the author's intent is clarified.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Try to identify the objection and the answer.
2
Understand the objection
The objection says that attributing to al-Qadi the view that neither power is attached to the other is far-fetched. Why? Because his actual position is that the eternal power attaches directly to both the act and its quality.
3
Analyze the answer
The answer reinterprets the condition: the phrase 'one of them attached to the other' means only one is attached, and the negation that is al-Qadi's school refers to a specific restriction (qayd), not to attachment altogether.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Qadi's view that neither power is attached to the other is an independent and far-fetched position.
Correction: Al-Qadi's view is actually a nuance within the case where one power is attached: the divine power attaches directly to the act and its quality, so the human power is not needed as an intermediate attachment.

Checkpoint
According to the gloss, what does al-Qadi's position actually mean?
A) The eternal power does not attach to the act at all
B) The eternal power attaches directly to both the essence and quality of the act
C) The human power is the sole influence
D) Neither power has any influence

Key Takeaway
Al-Qadi's view is not a separate case in the classification; it is a specific understanding of divine causality that fits within the existing framework.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological position that seems far-fetched, do you immediately dismiss it, or do you take the 'minimal attention' to understand it? Where in your life today can you extend this charitable interpretation to someone you disagree with?
?
Al-Qadi's view was defended by showing that his negation was not a blanket denial but a specific nuance. Is there a relationship—personal or intellectual—where you have made a blanket judgment without carefully distinguishing what the other person actually affirms and negates? Name it and commit to re-examining it this week.
?
This gloss reinterprets a phrase to include al-Qadi's view rather than exclude it. Are there people or ideas you have excluded from your understanding of Islam because you assumed they didn't fit? What would it take for you to revisit that exclusion with the 'minimal attention' shown here?

Defending al-Qadi's Inclusion in the Classification
Objection and answer defending al-Qadi's classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection against al-Qadi's inclusion in the classification is answered by clarifying the meaning of 'only one is subordinated'.

Core Idea
The objection is resolved by a precise reading of the phrase 'only one' in the classification.

The previous lesson presented al-Qadi's view as one of the possibilities in the classification of relations between two powers. Now an objection arises: how can al-Qadi's view be included when the classification assumes neither power is subordinated? The author provides a careful answer that hinges on the nuance of the word 'only'. This lesson unpacks that critical clarification.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the objector's argument: why is it problematic to include al-Qadi's school? Notice the assumption that neither power is subordinated to the other.
2
Identify the proposed reinterpretation
The objector suggests the author may have only been listing logical possibilities, not actual schools. See how this would undermine the classification's exclusivity.
3
Understand the answer
The answer clarifies that the classification's phrase 'one is subordinated to the other' actually means 'only one is subordinated'. This nuance includes al-Qadi's view because in his system, the eternal power is subordinated to the servant's power for both essence and attribute.
The key is the word 'فقط' (only).
4
Confirm the doctrinal detail
Notice that in al-Qadi's view, the eternal power relates to the servant's power and to the act's attribute directly, so the negation is only of the 'only' part. This preserves the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وهو مذهب القاضي قيل : هذا مشكل لأن المفروض عدم كون إحدى القدرتين متعلقة بالأخرى، أي أثر لها ونسبة هذا إلى القاضي بعيد فلا يبعد أن يقال : مقصود المصنف ضبط الاحتمالات العقلية، ومذهب القاضي مندرج فيما سبق والأخير مما لم يذهب إليه أحد إلا أن في الانحصار فيما ذكر نظراً. والجواب: يحتاج إلى أدنى عناية يشهد بها السياق، وهو أن يقال : المراد من قوله : فأما مع كون إحداهما متعلقة للأخرى أن يكون إحداهما فقط متعلقة بالأخرى أي لا يكون أصل الفعل ولا وصفه متعلقاً بها فالنفي الذي هو مذهب القاضي راجع إلى قيد فقط لأن القدرة القديمة ،عنده كما تتعلق بقدرة العبد على أصل الفعل تتعلق بوصف الفعل أيضاً ابتداء .
His saying: 'And it is the school of al-Qadi.' It is said: This is problematic because it is assumed that neither of the two powers is subordinated to the other, i.e., affected by it, and attributing this to al-Qadi is improbable. So it is not unlikely that it be said: The author's intention is to enumerate the rational possibilities, and al-Qadi's school is included in what preceded, and the last [possibility] is one that no one has held, but the exclusivity in what he mentioned is questionable. The answer: It requires minimal attention that the context bears witness, which is that it be said: The meaning of his saying 'as for when one of them is subordinated to the other' is that only one of them is subordinated to the other, i.e., neither the essence of the act nor its attribute is subordinated to it. So the negation which is the school of al-Qadi refers only to the qualification 'only', because the eternal power, according to him, just as it is subordinated to the servant's power concerning the essence of the act, it is also subordinated to the attribute of the act from the beginning.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection against the classification of al-Qadi's view in the previous rule. The objector argues that the classification is problematic because it assumes that in al-Qadi's view, neither power is subordinated to the other, meaning that one power does not cause the other. This attribution to al-Qadi is unlikely, so perhaps the author's aim was only to list logical possibilities, and al-Qadi's school actually falls under an earlier category, while the last possibility (neither subordinated) is held by no one. Thus, the claimed exclusivity of the classification is flawed. The answer clarifies that the author's intended meaning is more precise: when he says 'one of them is subordinated to the other,' he means that only one is subordinated to the other, i.e., neither the act's essence nor its attribute depends on the other power. The negation that characterizes al-Qadi's view applies only to the qualification 'only' because, in al-Qadi's system, the eternal power is subordinated to the servant's power regarding both the essence and the attribute of the act from the outset. Thus, the classification is correct when properly understood.

Key Terms
مذهب القاضي
تعلق إحداهما بالأخرى
أصل الفعل
وصف الفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that al-Qadi's view is excluded from the classification because it seems to assume mutual independence of powers.
Correction: The classification, when properly understood, includes al-Qadi's view because it means 'only one is subordinated,' which is exactly al-Qadi's position: the eternal power is subordinated to the servant's power for both essence and attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what does the phrase 'only one of them is subordinated to the other' mean in the context of al-Qadi's school?
A) One power is the cause of the other
B) Neither the essence nor the attribute of the act is subordinated to the other power
C) Both powers are completely independent
D) The servant's power is subordinated to God's power

Key Takeaway
The objection against al-Qadi's inclusion is resolved by understanding that the classification intends 'only one is subordinated,' which matches his doctrine of the eternal power's dual subordination.

First Argument: Servant's Action Possible and in God's Power
First proof that the servant's action is in God's power, with objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument for God's creation of human actions, its reliance on divine omnipotence and the impossibility of two effective powers, and the commentator's critique that the argument only establishes possibility, not actual occurrence.

Core Idea
The first argument seems to prove divine control, but only shows possibility, not actuality.

The Mawaqif begins its case for God's creation of human actions with a simple syllogism: the servant's action is possible, and God has power over all possible things, so the action must be in God's power. But the commentator al-Jurjani immediately raises a subtle objection: the earlier principle of divine power only covered possibility, not actual occurrence. This lesson walks through both the argument and its critical limitation.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and its premises
Begin by carefully reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the two premises: (1) the servant's action is possible, (2) every possible is within God's power. Note the conclusion that the action is in God's power.
The passage also asserts that nothing within God's power can be effected by the servant, due to the impossibility of two effective powers on one object.
2
Understand the commentator's objection
The commentator points out a flaw: the earlier discussion only established 'comprehensiveness of possibility' (shumūl al-jawāz), not 'comprehensiveness of occurrence' (shumūl al-wuqū'). The argument assumes that if God can do something, He actually does it, which is not necessarily true.
The objection relies on the 'proof of mutual exclusion' (dalīl al-tamānu'), which the commentator claims is not applicable between God and creature.
3
Reflect on the insufficiency of the argument
Consider why the first argument fails to prove its claim. The key distinction is between potential power (God can create the action) and actual creation (He does create it). The argument conflates the two.
This sets the stage for subsequent arguments in the Mawaqif.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الأول : إن فعل العبد ممكن في نفسه وكل ممكن مقدور لله تعالى لما مر من شمول قدرته للمكنات بأسرها، وقد مر الناس من المعتزلة والفرق الخارجة عن الإسلام في أن كل ممكن مقدور على تفاصيل مذاهبهم وإبطالها في بحث قادرية الله تعالى ولا شيء مما هو لله بواقع بقدرة العبد لامتناع اجتماع قدرتين مؤثرتين على مقدور واحد لما مر. قوله: (لما مر من شمول قدرته) فيه بحث وهو أن المذكور فيما مر شمول الجواز لا شمول الوقوع أعني أن يكون الكل واقعاً بالفعل بقدرته تعالى، ثم إن شمول الجواز إنما يستلزم شمول الوقوع إذا تم دليل التمانع لكن ما ذكره الشارح في بحث القدرة من هذا الموقف جواباً عن قول الجبائي يدفع جريان ذلك الدليل في الخالق والمخلوق فالوجه الأول لا يدل على المدعى.
The first aspect: Indeed the servant's action is possible in itself, and every possible is in the power of God Almighty due to what has passed of the comprehensiveness of His power over all possible things. And the people among the Mu'tazila and the sects outside Islam have passed in that every possible is in [God's] power concerning the details of their doctrines and their refutation in the discussion of God's omnipotence. And nothing which belongs to God occurs by the servant's power due to the impossibility of two effective powers concurring on one object of power, as has passed. His statement: 'due to what has passed of the comprehensiveness of His power' contains a discussion: that what is mentioned previously is the comprehensiveness of possibility, not the comprehensiveness of occurrence, meaning that everything actually occurs by His power. Then the comprehensiveness of possibility only entails the comprehensiveness of occurrence if the proof of mutual exclusion (dalīl al-tamānu') is complete. But what the commentator (al-Shāriḥ) mentioned in the discussion of power in this station as a response to the statement of al-Jubbāʾī repels the application of that proof between the Creator and the created. So the first aspect does not prove the claim.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument (al-wajh al-awwal) from the Mawaqif that the servant's action is created by God, not the servant. The argument begins by stating that the servant's voluntary action is possible in itself. Then it invokes a principle established earlier: every possible thing is within God's power. Therefore, the action is in God's power. Next, it asserts that nothing that is within God's power can occur by the servant's power, because two effective powers cannot concur on one object of power, as also established earlier. The commentator (al-Jurjani) then provides a marginal objection: the earlier discussion only established that every possible is permissible for God to create (shumūl al-jawāz), not that every possible actually occurs by His power (shumūl al-wuqū'). The entailment from possibility to actuality requires the proof of mutual exclusion (dalīl al-tamānu'), which is not applicable between God and creature according to al-Jurjani's earlier response to al-Jubbā'ī. Therefore, this first argument does not prove the claim.

Key Terms
الوجه الأول
ممكن
مقدور
قدرتين مؤثرتين
مقدور واحد
شمول الجواز
شمول الوقوع
دليل التمانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first argument is conclusive and proves that God creates human actions.
Correction: The commentator shows that the argument only demonstrates that the action is possible for God to create, not that He actually creates it. The distinction between potential and actual divine power is crucial.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator (al-Jurjani) point out about the first argument's use of 'comprehensiveness of power'?
A) It proves that God actually creates every possible thing.
B) It only establishes the possibility of creation, not actual occurrence.
C) It is invalid because God's power is limited.
D) It contradicts the Mu'tazilite position.

Key Takeaway
The first argument does not prove that God creates human actions; it only shows that God can potentially create them, but actual creation requires further proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because God can do something, He must do it? How might that assumption affect your understanding of why He allows certain actions to occur in your life? Reflect on one specific area where you might be confusing possibility with certainty.
?
The commentator's objection shows that a seemingly strong argument can have a subtle flaw. In your own faith journey, is there a belief you hold that you thought was proven, but upon closer examination might only be possible? What would it take for you to examine it honestly?
?
If the first argument does not prove that God creates your actions, then how do you understand your own responsibility for what you do? This lesson forces you to confront the gap between divine power and human accountability. How does this tension make you feel, and what do you need to reconcile within yourself?

The Eternal Preponderant as the Eternal Will
Clarification of the term 'eternal preponderant'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the 'eternal preponderant' in this argument refers to the eternal will, and how the commentator clarifies this point to reject a misinterpretation.

Core Idea
The eternal preponderant is the eternal will, not just any motive.

The commentator begins by explaining a key term in the argument: the 'eternal preponderant'. Some might think it is just any motive, but the commentator insists it refers specifically to the eternal will. This clarification is crucial for understanding the rest of the discussion about divine action and necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to see how the commentator introduces the term.
Notice the phrase 'فيه بحث' – there is a discussion.
2
Identify the key term
The commentator says 'المراد بالمرجح القديم هو الإرادة القديمة'. What does this mean?
3
Understand the objection being rejected
The commentator refers to the opponent's admission about the necessity of motive. Why does he reject the context?
He says 'فلا نسلم السياق'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بل ينتهي إلى مرجح (قديم فيه بحث أن المراد بالمرجح القديم هو الإرادة القديمة، كما دل عليه قوله فقد عرفت جوابه فلا نسلم السياق إذ قد تحققت باعترافه أن مبنى الكلام على وجوب الداعي في الفعل الاختياري . قوله : (بل عن غيره فلا يكون هو إلخ فيه بحث وهو أن انتفاء صدور المرجح عن العبد باختياره لا يستدعي صدوره عن غيره لجواز أن يكون صادراً عنه من غير اختياره بلا لزوم التسلسل وقولهم : كل حادث مسبوق بالاختيار متفرع على أن لا مؤثر في الوجود إلا الله تعالى وإلا فالإحراق الحادث يستند إلى النار الحادث بالإيجاب، وذلك هاهنا أول المسألة، اللهم إلا أن يبنى الكلام على اعتراف الخصم بصدوره عن غيره فتأمل . قوله : ( تطرق إليه شائبة الإيجاب ) يعني فلا فرق حينئذ فلا فرق حينئذ بين أفعال الله تعالى وأفعال العباد في الاضطرارية، نعم بينها فرق باعتبار أن الله تعالى مستقل في أفعاله لصدور المرجح عنه، والعبد ليس بمستقل في أفعاله لصدور المرجح عن غيره والحق أن الفعل وإن وجب بعد تعلق إرادته تعالى لكن لا يلزم الإيجاب لأن التعلق ليس بلازم لها بل هو مسبوق بتعلق آخر لا إلى نهاية كما
His saying: '(But it ends at an eternal preponderant') there is a discussion: the intended by 'eternal preponderant' is the eternal will, as indicated by his saying 'you have known its answer', so we do not accept the context, since it is established by his admission that the discourse is based on the necessity of the motive in voluntary action. His saying: '(But from other than him, so he is not, etc.') there is a discussion: the non-occurrence of the preponderant from the servant by his choice does not necessitate its occurrence from another, because it is possible that it occurs from him without his choice without necessitating infinite regress. Their statement 'every created thing is preceded by choice' is based on that there is no effect in existence except God; otherwise the created burning is attributed to the created fire by necessity, and that is here the beginning of the issue. Unless the discourse is based on the opponent's admission that it occurs from another, so reflect. His saying: '(The tinge of necessity attaches to it') means there is then no difference, there is then no difference between the actions of God and the actions of servants in terms of compulsion. Yes, there is a difference between them in that God is independent in His actions because the preponderant proceeds from Him, and the servant is not independent in his actions because the preponderant proceeds from another. The truth is that the act, although necessary after the attachment of His will, does not entail necessitation, because the attachment is not necessary for it, but rather is preceded by another attachment ad infinitum...
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on specific phrases from the previous argument. The commentator first clarifies that the 'eternal preponderant' refers to the eternal will, rejecting one implication. He then discusses the statement that the preponderant comes from another, arguing that the absence of the preponderant from the servant's choice does not force it to come from someone else, as it could come from him without choice without regress. He also critiques the principle that every created thing is preceded by choice, noting that it assumes that only God is the efficient cause. Finally, he explains the phrase about the 'tinge of necessity', stating that it does not mean complete compulsion; the difference remains: God is independent, the servant is not. The truth is that necessity after the will's attachment does not imply absolute determinism because the attachment is not necessary but preceded by another.

Key Terms
مرجح قديم
إرادة قديمة
داعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'eternal preponderant' refers to any cause or motive, not specifically the divine will.
Correction: The commentator explicitly states that it refers to the eternal will, which is the preponderant that makes God's actions voluntary but not compelled.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what does 'eternal preponderant' refer to?
A) A created motive
B) The eternal will
C) The servant's choice
D) A necessary cause

Key Takeaway
The eternal preponderant is the eternal will, not just any motive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you face trials, do you remember that Allah's will is eternal and preponderant, or do you feel that events are random? How can you strengthen your trust in His wise decree?
?
If the eternal preponderant is Allah's will alone, are there areas in your life where you still act as if your own motives or desires are ultimate? What would it look like to submit those to His will?

Necessity and Compulsion: Divine Independence
Refutation of the inference that necessity after will implies compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the necessity of an action after God's will does not imply compulsion, and how the argument about the preponderant from the servant shows the difference between God's independence and the servant's dependence.

Core Idea
Necessity after God's will is not compulsion; the will itself is not necessary.

This passage tackles an important objection: if God's act is necessary after His will, does that mean God is compelled? The commentator argues that it does not, because the attachment of the will is not necessary but preceded by another attachment ad infinitum. He also addresses the issue of the servant's action and the principle that every created thing is preceded by choice, showing that the argument against the opponent relies on specific assumptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to follow the discussion on the servant's preponderant and the principle of choice.
2
Analyze the argument about the servant
The commentator says that the non-occurrence of the preponderant from the servant by choice does not force it to come from another. Why?
Possible without choice without infinite regress.
3
Understand the critique of the principle
The principle 'every created thing is preceded by choice' is based on the assumption that only God is an efficient cause. How does he critique this?
4
Grasp the distinction between God and servant
The commentator concludes that there is no difference in terms of compulsion, but there is a difference in independence. How?
God is independent because preponderant from Him; servant depends on another.
5
Note the final truth
The act is necessary after the will's attachment, but that does not imply necessitation because the attachment is not necessary; it is preceded by another will ad infinitum.
This resolves the apparent determinism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بل ينتهي إلى مرجح (قديم فيه بحث أن المراد بالمرجح القديم هو الإرادة القديمة، كما دل عليه قوله فقد عرفت جوابه فلا نسلم السياق إذ قد تحققت باعترافه أن مبنى الكلام على وجوب الداعي في الفعل الاختياري . قوله : (بل عن غيره فلا يكون هو إلخ فيه بحث وهو أن انتفاء صدور المرجح عن العبد باختياره لا يستدعي صدوره عن غيره لجواز أن يكون صادراً عنه من غير اختياره بلا لزوم التسلسل وقولهم : كل حادث مسبوق بالاختيار متفرع على أن لا مؤثر في الوجود إلا الله تعالى وإلا فالإحراق الحادث يستند إلى النار الحادث بالإيجاب، وذلك هاهنا أول المسألة، اللهم إلا أن يبنى الكلام على اعتراف الخصم بصدوره عن غيره فتأمل . قوله : ( تطرق إليه شائبة الإيجاب ) يعني فلا فرق حينئذ فلا فرق حينئذ بين أفعال الله تعالى وأفعال العباد في الاضطرارية، نعم بينها فرق باعتبار أن الله تعالى مستقل في أفعاله لصدور المرجح عنه، والعبد ليس بمستقل في أفعاله لصدور المرجح عن غيره والحق أن الفعل وإن وجب بعد تعلق إرادته تعالى لكن لا يلزم الإيجاب لأن التعلق ليس بلازم لها بل هو مسبوق بتعلق آخر لا إلى نهاية كما
His saying: '(But it ends at an eternal preponderant') there is a discussion: the intended by 'eternal preponderant' is the eternal will, as indicated by his saying 'you have known its answer', so we do not accept the context, since it is established by his admission that the discourse is based on the necessity of the motive in voluntary action. His saying: '(But from other than him, so he is not, etc.') there is a discussion: the non-occurrence of the preponderant from the servant by his choice does not necessitate its occurrence from another, because it is possible that it occurs from him without his choice without necessitating infinite regress. Their statement 'every created thing is preceded by choice' is based on that there is no effect in existence except God; otherwise the created burning is attributed to the created fire by necessity, and that is here the beginning of the issue. Unless the discourse is based on the opponent's admission that it occurs from another, so reflect. Checkpoint
According to the commentator, does the necessity of an act after God's will imply that God is compelled?
A) Yes, it implies compulsion
