Refuting Tawlīd: The Dilemma of Two Agents
Al-Jurjani presents the Ash'ari refutation of tawlīd via a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari refutation of tawlīd by showing it leads to an impossibility.

Core Idea
Tawlīd forces a choice between two impossible options.

After defining tawlīd, al-Jurjani presents the Ash'ari refutation. The relied-upon argument is that all possibles depend directly on God. But he also offers a logical dilemma: if two agents act on one body, tawlīd implies either two independent agents on one act (impossible) or preponderance without a preponderant (impossible). This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Pay attention to the dilemma structure.
The key phrase is 'يلزم من التوليد إما اجتماع قادرين مستقلين على مقدور واحد، وإما الترجيح بلا مرجح'.
2
Visualize the example
Imagine a body touched by two agents, one pulling and one pushing simultaneously. The movement of the body is a single act.
3
Understand the reasoning
Why does tawlīd lead to the two impossible options? If the movement is generated, it must be from both (impossible: two agents on one act) or from one (impossible: preponderance without a reason).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

The refutation of tawlīd teaches that no act is generated from another—every act comes directly from Allah. How can you cultivate the heart to see that your good deeds are not merely the product of previous causes but directly from Allah's grace?
?
If you truly believe that every single act is directly from Allah, what changes in how you prepare for prayer, fight a bad habit, or seek repentance? Does this either free you from despair or increase your urgency to ask Him for everything?

Definition of Tawleed
Al-Iji defines tawleed and its motivation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite concept of tawleed (generation) and the motivation behind it.

Core Idea
Tawleed explains how one act can necessitate another without intention.

The Mu'tazila needed to explain how human acts can have unintended consequences while still being attributed to the agent. They proposed tawleed, meaning that one act can 'generate' another. This lesson presents their definition and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage defining tawleed and its translation. Notice the logical structure: why the Mu'tazila could not attribute sequential acts directly to human power.
The key phrase is 'يوجب فعل لفاعله فعلا آخر'.
2
Understand the problem
Identify why the Mu'tazila thought tawleed was necessary: they saw a sequence in human acts, and some acts follow even without intention, so they needed a mechanism.
3
Analyze the example
The example of hand and key: moving the hand generates movement of the key. This illustrates how a primary act necessitates a secondary act.
Notice that the agent may not intend the key's movement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن المعتزلة لما أسندوا أفعال العباد إليهم ورأوا فيه ترتباً ورأوا فيها أيضاً أن الفعل المترتب على آخر يصدر عنهم وإن لم يقصدوا إليه أصلاً، فلم يمكنهم لهذا إسناد الفعل المترتب إلى تأثير قدرتهم فيه ابتداء لتوقفه على القصد قالوا بالتوليد وهو أن يوجب فعل لفاعله فعلا آخر نحو حركة اليد وحركة المفتاح، فإن الأولى منهما أوجبت لفاعلها الثانية سواء قصدها أو لم يقصدها والمعتمد في إبطاله ما بينا استناد الممكنات إلى الله تعالى ابتداء وقد يحتج عليه بأنه يلزم من التوليد إما اجتماع قادرين مستقلين على مقدور واحد، وإما الترجيح بلا مرجح وذلك لأنه إذا التصق جسم بكف قادرين وجذبه أحدهما ودفعه الآخر في زمان جذبه إلى جهته فإن قلنا : حركته تولدت من حركة اليد فإما بهما أي بالجذب والدفع معا فيلزم مقدور بین قادرين مستقلين بالتأثير وقد استحالته، وإما بأحدهما وهو تحكم محض معلوم بطلانه
Know that when the Mu'tazila attributed the acts of servants to themselves and saw in them a sequence, and also saw that the act which follows from another issues from them even if they did not intend it at all, they were unable for this reason to attribute the sequential act to the effect of their power in it initially because it depends on intention. So they said: generation (tawleed), which is that an act necessitates for its agent another act, like the movement of the hand and the movement of the key; for the first of them necessitates for its agent the second, whether he intended it or not. And the relied-upon argument in refuting it is what we have explained: the dependence of all possible things on God Almighty initially. And it may be argued against it by saying that generation necessitates either the meeting of two independent agents on a single object of power, or preponderance without a preponderant. That is because if a body is attached to the palm of two agents, one pulling it and the other pushing it at the time of pulling it to his direction, then if we say: its movement was generated from the movement of the hand, then either by both—that is, by the pulling and pushing together—so an object of power between two independent agents would be necessitated, which is impossible; or by one of them—which is pure arbitrary judgment, known to be false.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage defines the Mu'tazilite concept of 'tawleed' (generation) and then presents a proof to refute it. The definition: tawleed is when an act (e.g., moving one's hand) necessarily causes another act (e.g., moving a key) for the same agent, even if the agent did not intend the second act. The Mu'tazila used this to explain how human acts can have effects without direct intention, preserving human agency. The refutation is twofold: first, the general principle that all possible things depend directly on God; second, a specific logical argument using the example of a body simultaneously pulled and pushed by two agents. If the body's motion is 'generated' from one of the hand movements, then either (a) both agents cause it together, implying two independent agents on one effect (impossible), or (b) only one agent causes it, but then why one rather than the other? That would be arbitrary (tahakkum). Thus tawleed is impossible. The proof is structured as a conditional: if generation occurred, then either impossible consequence follows, so generation is false.

Key Terms
التوليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the Mu'tazila propose tawleed?
A) To deny human free will
B) To explain how acts can be caused without intention
C) To prove that God creates all acts
D) To defend the concept of secondary causes

Key Takeaway
Tawleed is the theory that an act can necessitate another act for the same agent, even without intention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

In light of the Mu'tazilite theory of tawleed, where do you locate the moral weight—on the intention or the generated act? Let this lesson confront you: do you excuse yourself by saying 'I didn't mean it,' while knowing the sequence was set in motion by your own hand?
?
The Mu'tazila introduced tawleed to preserve human agency in unintended acts. But ask yourself: are you quick to claim credit for good unintended outcomes but deny responsibility for bad ones? How does your heart react to the idea that your actions generate consequences that are inseparably yours, whether you intended them or not? Will you now begin to own the full chain of your actions?
?
Imagine standing before Allah with a book of deeds containing actions you never intended—generated acts. Does the Mu'tazilite solution comfort you, or does it unsettle your sense of justice? What does that reveal about your actual trust in Allah's justice versus your own calculations? Let this reflection lead you to repent of hiding behind 'I didn't mean it' and instead take full responsibility for every ripple of your choices.

Proof Against Tawleed
Al-Iji presents a logical proof refuting tawleed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument used to refute tawleed, including the dilemma between two independent agents and arbitrary preference.

Core Idea
The proof shows tawleed leads to an impossible consequence: either two agents on one effect or choice without reason.

After defining tawleed, al-Iji refutes it with a logical argument. The proof uses a thought experiment: two agents simultaneously pulling and pushing a body. If the body's motion is 'generated' from one hand's movement, we face an impossible choice. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic text of the proof and its translation. Note that it begins with 'والمعتمد في إبطاله' (the relied-upon argument in refuting it).
There is a brief reference to a prior argument; focus on the detailed proof.
2
Set up the scenario
Understand the example: two agents, one pulling and one pushing a body at the same time. The body's motion is in question.
3
Identify the two possibilities
The text says: if the motion is generated from the hand movement, it must be either from both hands together or from one hand alone. Recognize these as the two horns of the dilemma.
4
Evaluate each possibility
The first possibility (both together) implies two independent agents on one effect – impossible. The second (one hand alone) is arbitrary because there is no reason to prefer one over the other.
This is pure tahakkum (arbitrary judgment).
5
Conclude the refutation
Since both possibilities are impossible, the assumption of tawleed must be false. The proof is a reductio ad absurdum.
The argument does not rely on divine intervention; it is purely logical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن المعتزلة لما أسندوا أفعال العباد إليهم ورأوا فيه ترتباً ورأوا فيها أيضاً أن الفعل المترتب على آخر يصدر عنهم وإن لم يقصدوا إليه أصلاً، فلم يمكنهم لهذا إسناد الفعل المترتب إلى تأثير قدرتهم فيه ابتداء لتوقفه على القصد قالوا بالتوليد وهو أن يوجب فعل لفاعله فعلا آخر نحو حركة اليد وحركة المفتاح، فإن الأولى منهما أوجبت لفاعلها الثانية سواء قصدها أو لم يقصدها والمعتمد في إبطاله ما بينا استناد الممكنات إلى الله تعالى ابتداء وقد يحتج عليه بأنه يلزم من التوليد إما اجتماع قادرين مستقلين على مقدور واحد، وإما الترجيح بلا مرجح وذلك لأنه إذا التصق جسم بكف قادرين وجذبه أحدهما ودفعه الآخر في زمان جذبه إلى جهته فإن قلنا : حركته تولدت من حركة اليد فإما بهما أي بالجذب والدفع معا فيلزم مقدور بین قادرين مستقلين بالتأثير وقد استحالته، وإما بأحدهما وهو تحكم محض معلوم بطلانه
Know that when the Mu'tazila attributed the acts of servants to themselves and saw in them a sequence, and also saw that the act which follows from another issues from them even if they did not intend it at all, they were unable for this reason to attribute the sequential act to the effect of their power in it initially because it depends on intention. So they said: generation (tawleed), which is that an act necessitates for its agent another act, like the movement of the hand and the movement of the key; for the first of them necessitates for its agent the second, whether he intended it or not. And the relied-upon argument in refuting it is what we have explained: the dependence of all possible things on God Almighty initially. And it may be argued against it by saying that generation necessitates either the meeting of two independent agents on a single object of power, or preponderance without a preponderant. That is because if a body is attached to the palm of two agents, one pulling it and the other pushing it at the time of pulling it to his direction, then if we say: its movement was generated from the movement of the hand, then either by both—that is, by the pulling and pushing together—so an object of power between two independent agents would be necessitated, which is impossible; or by one of them—which is pure arbitrary judgment, known to be false.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage defines the Mu'tazilite concept of 'tawleed' (generation) and then presents a proof to refute it. The definition: tawleed is when an act (e.g., moving one's hand) necessarily causes another act (e.g., moving a key) for the same agent, even if the agent did not intend the second act. The Mu'tazila used this to explain how human acts can have effects without direct intention, preserving human agency. The refutation is twofold: first, the general principle that all possible things depend directly on God; second, a specific logical argument using the example of a body simultaneously pulled and pushed by two agents. If the body's motion is 'generated' from one of the hand movements, then either (a) both agents cause it together, implying two independent agents on one effect (impossible), or (b) only one agent causes it, but then why one rather than the other? That would be arbitrary (tahakkum). Thus tawleed is impossible. The proof is structured as a conditional: if generation occurred, then either impossible consequence follows, so generation is false.

Key Terms
قادرين مستقلين
مقدور واحد
الترجيح بلا مرجح
تحكم محض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof only applies to physical motions and not to other types of generated acts.
Correction: The proof is meant to show the logical impossibility of any generated act because it would entail either conflicting causality or arbitrary choice, regardless of the type of act.

Checkpoint
In the proof against tawleed, what is the consequence of saying the body's motion is generated from the movement of a single hand?
A) It implies two independent agents on one effect
B) It implies arbitrary preference without cause
C) It denies human power entirely
D) It leads to infinite regress

Key Takeaway
The proof shows that tawleed leads to an impossible dilemma: either two independent agents on one effect or an arbitrary choice, both of which are rationally untenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something through your own efforts, do you genuinely feel that God alone created that outcome, or is there a subtle sense that you 'generated' it yourself? How does this proof challenge the way you attribute causation in your daily life?
?
Consider a choice you made recently where you had no compelling reason for your decision—perhaps out of habit or whim. Does this proof's rejection of 'pure arbitrary judgment' prompt you to examine such choices? Will you commit to grounding decisions in clear reasoning or divine guidance from now on?
?
This proof shows that attributing any effect to a created cause as a genuine generator leads to logical absurdity. How does this affect your view of secondary causes in your own life? Are you willing to shift from seeing yourself as a 'co-creator' of events to seeing yourself as a mere channel through which God acts? What practical change would that demand today?

The Exception of Dirar and Hafs on Generated Acts
Al-Iji explains the exception of Dirar and Hafs and their classification of generated acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proof of impossibility of generation does not apply to Dirar and Hafs and how they classify generated acts based on the locus of power.

Core Idea
Dirar and Hafs deny generation for acts outside the agent's locus of power.

In this passage, al-Iji explains that the earlier refutation of tawleed does not apply to Dirar ibn 'Amr and Hafs al-Fard because they hold a nuanced view. They distinguish between generated acts that subsist in the agent's locus of power and those that do not, and only consider the latter as the agent's act if they occur in accordance with his choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall argument.
2
Identify the key claim
Notice that the proof fails for Dirar and Hafs because they reject generation for acts outside the locus of power.
3
Understand the classification
The text divides generated acts into two categories: those within the locus of power and those outside. Dirar and Hafs have different rulings for each.
4
Examine the examples
The examples of cutting and slaughtering illustrate when the act is considered the agent's even if the tool is separate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الاحتجاج الدال على لزوم المحال للتوليد في المثال المذكور لا يلزم ضراراً وحفصاً القائلين بعدم التوليد فيما قام بغير محل القدرة، وبيانه على ما في الأبكار أن المتولدات منها ما هي قائمة بمحل القدرة كالعلم النظري المتولد من النظر ومنها ما هي قائمة بغير محل القدرة، فاختلفت المعتزلة، فذهب بعضهم إلى أنها بأسرها فعل الفاعل السبب وإن كان معدوماً حال وجود المتولد كمن رمى سهما ومات قبل بلوغ السهم الرمية فإن الإصابة والآلام الحادثة منها من فعل الميت وذهب ثمامة بن أسرش إلى أنها كلها حوادث لا محدث لها والنظام إلى أن المتولدات برمتها من فعل الله تعالى لا من فعل العبد الفاعل للسبب وذهب ضرار بن عمرو وحفص الفرد إلى أن ما كان منها في محل قدرة الفاعل فهو من فعله وما كان في محل مباين لمحلها فما وقع منه على وفق اختياره فهو أيضاً من فعله كالقطع والذبح وما لا يقع على وفقه فليس من فعله كالآلام في المضروب والاندفاع والثقيل المدفوع وحركة الجسم المفروض من القسم الأخير
And this proof, which indicates the impossibility of generation in the mentioned example, does not apply to Dirar and Hafs, who deny generation for what subsists in other than the locus of power. Its explanation according to what is in al-Abkar is that generated things include those that subsist in the locus of power, like theoretical knowledge generated from reflection, and those that subsist in other than the locus of power. The Mu'tazila differed: some of them held that all of them are the act of the agent of the cause even if he is non-existent at the time of the generated thing's existence—like one who shoots an arrow and dies before the arrow reaches the target, for the injury and pains occurring from it are the act of the dead person. Thumama ibn Ashras held that all of them are events with no originator. al-Nazzam held that generated things entirely are the act of God Almighty, not the act of the servant who is the agent of the cause. Dirar ibn 'Amr and Hafs al-Fard held that what is in the locus of the agent's power is his act, and what is in a locus separate from it: if it occurs in accordance with his choice, it is also his act, like cutting and slaughtering; and if it does not occur in accordance with his choice, it is not his act, like pains in the beaten and the momentum of the heavy pushed object and the movement of the supposed body from the last category.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage clarifies that the earlier refutation of tawleed does not apply to Dirar ibn 'Amr and Hafs al-Fard, because they restrict generation to acts that subsist in the locus of the agent's power. The text then explains their view using material from the work al-Abkar. Generated acts are divided into two types: (1) those subsisting in the agent's locus of power (e.g., theoretical knowledge generated from reflection), and (2) those subsisting outside it. For the latter, the Mu'tazila held various opinions: some said the effect is the act of the original agent even when he is dead (like Thumama's view that all generated acts are events with no originator), al-Nazzam said all generated acts are God's act, and Dirar and Hafs took a middle position: if the effect occurs in accordance with the agent's choice (e.g., cutting or slaughtering by a knife), it is the agent's act; if it does not (e.g., pain or momentum that continues after the agent's death), it is not. The example of the body in the earlier proof falls into the last category, so the proof does not refute their position. The passage thus provides a comprehensive survey of Mu'tazilite theories of causality and agency.

Key Terms
ضرار بن عمرو
حفص الفرد
محل القدرة
المتولدات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Dirar and Hafs completely deny any human causal influence on generated acts.
Correction: In fact, they attribute some generated acts to the human agent when they occur in accordance with his choice, such as cutting or slaughtering.

Checkpoint

A) Always, regardless of choice
B) Never, it is God's act
C) Only if it occurs in accordance with the agent's choice
D) Only if the agent is still alive

Key Takeaway
Dirar and Hafs argue that generated acts outside the agent's locus of power are only attributable to him if they align with his choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a situation today where you caused an effect indirectly—perhaps a word you spoke led to a chain of reactions. According to Dirar and Hafs, if that effect aligns with your intention, it is attributable to you. Does your conscience hold you accountable for that indirect effect? If not, will you today consciously acknowledge and take responsibility for one such indirect consequence of your actions?
?
Consider an act you performed out of habit that produced an unintended outcome. Dirar and Hafs would not attribute that outcome to you because it did not occur in accordance with your choice. Reflect on a specific moment where you distanced yourself from an unintended effect. Does this alignment with your choice truly define moral responsibility? Will you examine your heart and ask yourself whether you are using lack of intention as an excuse to avoid accountability in some area of your life?
?
The classification of Dirar and Hafs forces you to consider: what is the role of your will in the acts you are held accountable for? If an effect is generated outside your direct power but aligns with your choice, you own it. Is there a 'generated act' in your life—a consequence you did not directly cause but that followed from a choice—that you have been ignoring? Commit today to addressing that consequence, whether it be in your family, work, or community.

Mu'tazilite Positions on Generated Acts
Al-Iji surveys the differing Mu'tazilite positions on the causal origin of generated acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the major Mu'tazilite positions on who causes generated acts, including the views of Thumama, al-Nazzam, and the mainstream.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilites disagreed sharply on the causal origin of generated acts.

After discussing Dirar and Hafs, al-Iji provides a survey of the broader Mu'tazilite spectrum on generated acts. Some held that the original agent is the cause even after death, Thumama said they have no originator, and al-Nazzam attributed them entirely to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to identify each distinct view.
2
List the views
There are three main views: (1) the mainstream view that the effect is the act of the original agent; (2) Thumama's view that they are events without an originator; (3) al-Nazzam's view that they are God's act.
Note also the example of the arrow shooter to illustrate the first view.
3
Understand the implications
Each view has implications for human responsibility and divine causality, though the text does not elaborate here.

Classification of Generated Acts and Mu'tazilite Opinions
Enumerates two types of generated acts and four Mu'tazilite opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two categories of generated acts and the four main Mu'tazilite positions on their attribution.

Core Idea
Generated acts are split by locus; scholars differ on whether they are from the agent, God, or no originator.

The Mu'tazila debated whether acts like the pain from a blow or the cutting from a knife are caused by the human agent or by God. This lesson introduces the foundational classification of generated acts and the spectrum of opinions among leading thinkers.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of generated acts
Identify the two types: those in the locus of power and those outside it.
This distinction is key to understanding the debate.
2
Learn the four opinions
Note the views: all from agent (some Mu'tazila), from no originator (Thumama), from God (al-Nazzam), and the nuanced view of Dirar and Hafs.
Each view has different implications for human responsibility.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this debate matters for free will and divine action.
This connects to broader kalam theology.

فهو أيضاً من فعله كالقطع والذبح وما لا يقع على وفقه فليس من فعله كالآلام في المضروب والاندفاع والثقيل المدفوع وحركة الجسم المفروض من القسم الأخير الأمر أنه مستقل بإحداث حركة ذلك الجسم في الجملة فحينئذ لا توارد ولا تحكم. قوله : ( وبيانه على ما في الابكار إلخ إنما قال على ما في الأبكار لما في تقرير مذهب ضرار وحفص من الاختلافات فإن ما نسب إليهما هاهنا من القول بعدم التوليد فيما قام بغير محل القدرة نسبه الشهرستاني في كتاب الملل والنحل إلى النظام بحكاية الكعبي عنه، ونسب إلى ضرار وحفص القول بكون أفعال العباد مخلوقة لله تعالى مكتسبة للعبد . قوله : ( وإن كان معدوماً حال وجود المتولد ) فلم يشترطوا بقاء الفاعل عند وجود الفعل بل اكتفوا في تحقق تمام العلة بوجود الفاعل في الجملة مقدماً أو مقارناً، فمعنى كون المتولد فعلاً للمعدوم مقدوراً له تأثيره باختياره في السبب الموجب له فلا يرد أن المتولد ولو كان مقدوراً للعبد لما وجد بعد فنائه . قوله : ( حوادث لا محدث لها أورد عليه أنه يستلزم استغناء العالم عن الصانع، وأجيب بأنهم إنما جوزوا ذلك في المتولدات لثبوت ما يفضي إلى حدوثها، ولا يجوزونه في غيرها فلا يلزم ذلك . قوله : ( كالقطع والذبح أراد بهما المقطوعية والمذبوحية لا القاطعية والذابحية فإنهما قائمان بمحل القدرة، ثم الفرق بينهما وبين الألم في المضروب والاندفاع في الثقيل حيث حكم بأن الأولين واقعان على وفق الاختيار دون الآخرين ما سيجيء من أن الآخرين قد يقعان بعد عجز فاعل السبب وموته فلا يكونان حينئذ على حسب القصد والداعية بخلاف الأولين، فإن قلت : قد يقع الأولان أيضاً بعد ذلك كما إذا رمى بسكين فاتفق بعد موت الرامي أن أصاب المذبح أو عضواً فحصل الذبح أو القطع قلت هذا ليس بذبح وقطع في العرف ولو سلم فالمعدود مما وقع على وفق الاختيار ليس القطع والذبح في مثل هذه الصورة ولا مطلقهما . فالزام بها لا يقوم بها حجة عليهما
The Sixth Observatory - the Second Intention: On Generation (al-Tawleed) and Its Branches. (And it is pure arbitrary judgment whose falsity is known) (And this proof indicating the necessity of absurdity for generation in the mentioned example) does not apply to Dirar and Hafs, who say there is no generation in what occurs in other than the locus of power. Its explanation according to al-Abkar: indeed the generated things (al-mutawallidat) are of two kinds: those that subsist in the locus of power, like theoretical knowledge generated from reflection, and those that subsist in other than the locus of power. The Mu'tazila differed: some held that all of them are the act of the agent who is the cause even if he is non-existent at the time of the generated thing's existence, like one who shoots an arrow and dies before the arrow reaches the target—the injury and pain resulting from it are the act of the dead person. Thumama ibn Ashras held that they are all events (hawadith) with no originator. Al-Nazzam held that all generated things are from God, not from the servant who is the cause of the means. Dirar ibn Amr and Hafs al-Fard held: whatever of them is in the locus of the agent's power is his act; whatever is in a locus distinct from its locus, if it occurs in accordance with his choice, it is also his act—like cutting (al-qat) and slaughtering (al-dhabh)—and what does not occur in accordance with his choice is not his act—like pain in the struck person, the impetus of the heavy object, and the movement of the body in question from the last category. The matter is that he is independent in causing the movement of that body in general; then there is no interference (tawarud) nor arbitrary judgment. His statement: 'And its explanation according to al-Abkar...' he only said 'according to al-Abkar' because of the differences in the formulation of the doctrine of Dirar and Hafs: what is attributed to them here—the denial of generation in what subsists in other than the locus of power—al-Shahrastani in the book al-Milal wa al-Nihal attributed to al-Nazzam, on the authority of al-Ka'bi, and attributed to Dirar and Hafs the doctrine that the acts of servants are created by God and acquired by the servant. His statement: 'even if he is non-existent at the time of the generated thing's existence'—they did not require the agent to remain at the existence of the act; rather they were satisfied for the attainment of the complete cause with the existence of the agent in general, prior or concurrent. So the meaning of the generated thing being an act of the non-existent and within his power is his effective influence by his choice in the means necessitating it; thus it is not objected that the generated thing, even if it were within the servant's power, would not exist after his annihilation. His statement: 'events with no originator'—it was objected that this necessitates the world's independence from the Maker; and it was answered: they only permitted that in generated things due to the existence of what leads to their occurrence, and they do not permit it in other things, so that does not follow. His statement: 'like cutting and slaughtering'—he meant by them being cut and being slaughtered, not the act of cutting and the act of slaughtering, for these two subsist in the locus of power. Then the difference between them and the pain in the struck person and the impetus in the heavy object, where he judged that the first two occur in accordance with choice unlike the others, is what will come: that the others may occur after the incapacity and death of the agent of the cause, so then they are not in accordance with intention and motive, unlike the first two. If you say: the first two may also occur after that, as when one throws a knife and after the thrower's death it happens to hit the throat or a limb and slaughter or cutting occurs; I say: this is not [real] slaughter or cutting in customary usage, and even if conceded, what is counted as occurring in accordance with choice is not the cutting and slaughter in such a case, nor their absolute. So the objection does not stand as a proof against them.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage classifies generated acts (al-mutawallidat) and presents the differing opinions of the Mu'tazila. It begins by stating that the proof of generation does not apply to Dirar and Hafs, who deny generation in acts that occur outside the locus of power. The author then, following al-Abkar, divides generated acts into two types: those that subsist in the locus of the agent's power (like theoretical knowledge from reflection) and those that subsist elsewhere. Among the Mu'tazila, some (e.g., Abu al-Husayn and followers) hold that all generated acts are the act of the agent even if the agent dies before the effect (like an arrow shooter dying before impact—the injury is attributed to him). Thumama says they are events without an originator. Al-Nazzam says all are from God. Dirar and Hafs distinguish: if the generated act is in the locus of power, it is the agent's act; if not, then if it occurs according to his choice (like cutting and slaughter), it is his act; otherwise (like pain) it is not. The commentary then explains each part: the attribution to Dirar and Hafs is disputed (al-Shahristani attributes their view to al-Nazzam, and their actual view is that acts are created by God and acquired by the servant). The condition of the agent's existence at the time of the generated act is discussed: they do not require simultaneity; the agent's prior or concurrent existence suffices for causality. The objection to Thumama's view (events without originator) is answered: such a view is allowed only for generated acts due to their clear causal sequences, not for all things. Finally, the distinction between cutting/slaughtering and pain/impetus is clarified: the former occur according to choice because they require intention, while the latter can occur after the agent's death without his intention. A possible objection (that cutting could also occur after death) is rebutted by custom and by limiting the scope.

Key Terms
توليد (tawleed)
متولد (mutawallid)
محل القدرة (mahall al-qudra)
ضرار (Dirar)
حفص (Hafs)
ثمامة (Thumama)
نظام (al-Nazzam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which Mu'tazilite held that generated acts are events with no originator?
A) Dirar ibn Amr
B) Hafs al-Fard
C) Thumama ibn Ashras
D) Al-Nazzam

Key Takeaway
The classification of generated acts into those in/outside the locus of power forms the basis for the Mu'tazilite debate on human causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accidentally cause harm to someone (like hurting their feelings or breaking something), do you instinctively feel responsible even if you did not intend the outcome? How does the Mu'tazilite debate on generated acts—especially Dirar and Hafs's condition of 'according to his choice'—challenge or affirm your sense of moral responsibility before God? What is one relationship or interaction where you need to take greater ownership of unintended consequences?
?
The passage shows that some scholars (like Thumama) believed generated acts have no originator. Think of a situation where something bad happened 'through' your action but you felt it was not really 'from' you—like a car accident where you had no time to react. In that moment, did you search for someone to blame? How does the theological position of 'no originator' sit with your heart? Are there events in your life you have not truly assigned to God's decree, leaving you in a state of doubt or resentment?
?
Al-Nazzam attributed all generated acts to God alone. Imagine your most cherished achievement—a successful project, a good deed—now consider that according to this view, the effect is entirely from God, not from you. Does that thought bring you humility or discomfort? How can you genuinely thank God for the outcomes of your efforts while still striving with your own responsibility? Name one success you need to reattribute to God today.

Causality and Created Things
advanced
8 min
Dirar and Hafs on the Conditions of Generated Acts
Explains and defends Dirar and Hafs' position on generated acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced position of Dirar and Hafs on generated acts, including the conditions of locus, choice, and the agent's existence.

Core Idea
Dirar and Hafs accept generation only when the act aligns with the agent's choice and the agent's causal influence is intact.

Dirar and Hafs provide a detailed answer to the problem of generated acts by distinguishing acts based on their locus and whether they occur according to the agent's choice. This lesson explores their reasoning, the condition of the agent's existence, and the objections raised against their view.

Guided Path
1
Review Dirar and Hafs' main claim
Re-read their position: acts in locus of power are always the agent's; acts outside are only if according to choice.
2
Understand the condition of the agent's existence
Learn that they do not require the agent to be alive at the moment of the effect; prior or concurrent existence suffices for causality.
This addresses the arrow-shooter example.
3
Distinguish between examples: cutting vs pain
Notice why cutting is considered according to choice while pain may not be.
4
Consider the objection and response
Reflect on the objection that cutting could also occur after death, and the rebuttal based on customary usage.
5
Synthesize
Summarize the criteria Dirar and Hafs use to attribute generated acts.

Source Text

المرصد السادس - المقصد الثاني : في التوليد وفروعه ( وهو تحكم محض معلوم بطلانه (وهذا الاحتجاج الدال على لزوم المحال للتوليد في المثال المذكور ) لا يلزم ضراراً وحفصاً القائلين بعدم التوليد فيما قام بغير محل القدرة ) ، وبيانه على ما في الأبكار أن المتولدات منها ما هي قائمة بمحل القدرة كالعلم النظري المتولد من النظر ومنها ما هي قائمة بغير محل القدرة، فاختلفت المعتزلة، فذهب بعضهم إلى أنها بأسرها فعل Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dirar and Hafs completely deny all generated acts.
Correction: They deny only those that are not in the locus of power and not according to choice; they accept others as the agent's act.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for Dirar and Hafs to consider a generated act as the agent's act when it is outside the locus of power?
A) The agent must be alive at the time of the act
B) The act must occur according to the agent's choice
C) The act must be within the locus of power
D) The agent must be the direct cause

Key Takeaway
Dirar and Hafs develop a nuanced causality: generated acts are attributed to the agent only when the act aligns with his choice and his causal role is intact.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action that requires your choice, do you genuinely feel that you are the cause, or do you attribute it entirely to God's creation? How does Dirar and Hafs' view of conditional generation challenge you to take responsibility for your choices?
?
Consider a situation where you caused harm unintentionally after losing control (e.g., a car accident). According to the lesson, does that act belong to you? Why does that distinction matter for your sense of moral accountability before God?
?
The lesson distinguishes acts that align with choice from those that don't. Is there an area of your life where you act without reflection, as if on autopilot, and then excuse yourself by saying 'it was not by choice'? What would it mean to reclaim that action as your own?

Causality and Created Things

The Mu'tazila's Intuitive and Inferential Arguments for Generated Acts
Mu'tazila present two arguments: intuitive (stone example) and inferential (capacity variation).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two key arguments the Mu'tazila used to support their claim that generated acts are attributed to human servants.

Core Idea
Generated acts are known both intuitively and by reasoning from human capacity differences.

This passage presents the Mu'tazila's strategy to prove that generated acts (like the movement of a pushed stone) come from human power. They offer both an intuitive claim, exemplified by the stone-pushing case, and an inferential argument based on the variation of human capacities. These two lines of reasoning aim to show that such acts cannot be directly from God but must be caused by the servant's power through intermediate acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two distinct arguments introduced by the terms "ضرورة" and "استدلال".
2
Identify the two claims
The passage presents two aspects: an intuitive claim (stone example) and an inferential claim (capacity variation). Identify each and note how they are elaborated.
Al-Amidi frames these as the first and second aspects of their proofs.
3
Examine the stone example
Focus on the example of pushing a stone: how does it illustrate the idea of a generated act? Why is the movement not a direct act?
The movement is according to the pusher's intention and will, and is agreed not to be a direct act.
4
Understand the capacity argument
Why does the difference in the capacities of a strong and weak person support the attribution of generated acts to human power? What would be the consequence if generated acts were from God directly?
The consequence is described as 'mukabara' (stubbornness), an absurdity.
5
Connect to the doctrine
Reflect on how these arguments serve the Mu'tazila's overall position that acts are generated from human power, not directly from God.

والمعتزلة ( القائلون بإسناد المتولدات إلى العباد ( ادعوا الضرورة تارة ( كأبي الحسين وأتباعه ) وجنحوا إلى الاستدلال أخرى كالجمهور منهم ( أما الضرورة فقالوا : من رام دفع حجر في جهة اندفع إليها بحسب قصده وإرادته ( فيكون اندفاعه صادراً عن الدافع وفعلاً له ، ( وليس ) هذا (الاندفاع) فعلاً له ( مباشراً بالاتفاق ) منا ومنكم ) فهو بواسطة ما باشره من الدفع ومتولد منه وكذا الكلام في حصول العلم النظري من النظر وحصول أمثاله من أسبابها، واعلم أن الآمدي جعل اندفاع الحجر على حسب قصده وإرادته وجها أول من وجوه استدلالاتهم، وليس في كلامه ما يدل على أن أبا الحسين ادعى الضرورة هاهنا ( ويؤيده اختلاف (الأفعال التي سميت متولدة باختلاف القدر الثابتة للعباد ( فالأيد ( القوي ( يقوى على حمل ما لا يقوى على حمله الضعيف ولو كان الفعل المتولد ( واقعاً بقدرة الله لجاز تحرك الجبل باعتماد الضعيف النحيف وعدم تحرك الخردلة باعتماد الأيد القوي بأن يخلق الله الحركة في الجبل دون الخردلة ( وأنه مكابرة ( صرفة فاتضح أن المتولدات مستندة إلى القدرة الحادثة لا مباشرة بل بتوسط أفعال أخر، والآمدي جعل هذا التأييد وجهاً ثانياً من دلائلهم
And the Mu'tazila—those who attribute generated acts to servants—claimed intuitive knowledge (al-darura) sometimes (like Abu al-Husayn and his followers) and inclined to inferential reasoning (al-istidlal) other times, like the majority of them. As for intuitive knowledge, they said: whoever intends to push a stone in a direction, it moves in that direction according to his intention and will; so its movement proceeds from the pusher and is his act. And this movement is not his direct act (mubashiran) by agreement of us and you; so it is by means of what he directly did of pushing and is generated (mutawallid) from it. The same applies to the attainment of theoretical knowledge from reflection and the attainment of similar things from their causes. Know that al-Amidi made the movement of the stone according to his intention and will a first aspect of their arguments, and there is nothing in his words indicating that Abu al-Husayn claimed intuitive knowledge here. And it is supported by the difference of the acts called generated according to the difference of the powers established for servants: the strong one is able to carry what the weak one cannot carry. If the generated act were occurring by God's power, it would be permissible for a mountain to move by the reliance of the weak lean person and for a mustard seed not to move by the reliance of the strong person—by God creating movement in the mountain but not in the mustard seed—and that is pure stubbornness (mukabara). So it became clear that generated acts are dependent on created power (al-qudra al-haditha), not directly but through the mediation of other acts. And al-Amidi made this support a second aspect of their proofs.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila's claim that knowledge of generated acts is intuitive (daruri) for some of them, like Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and his followers, while others rely on inferential reasoning. The intuitive argument is illustrated by the example of pushing a stone: when a person intends to push a stone, it moves in the direction of his intention. Everyone agrees this movement is not the direct act of the pusher, but it is generated from his direct pushing. Similarly, theoretical knowledge comes from reflection. The author notes that al-Amidi considered the stone example as a first aspect of their arguments, but does not claim that Abu al-Husayn asserted intuition here. Then a supporting argument is given: acts vary with the capacity of agents (a strong person can carry heavy loads, a weak person cannot). If generated acts were directly from God, then the weak person's relying on a mountain could move it, and the strong person's relying on a mustard seed might not move it—since God could create movement in the mountain and not in the seed. This is absurd (mukabara). Therefore, generated acts must be caused by the servant's created power, indirectly through other acts. Al-Amidi calls this a second aspect.

Key Terms
ضرورة (darura)
استدلال (istidlal)
دفع حجر (daf' hajar)
اندفاع (indifa')
قدرة حادثة (qudra haditha)
مكابرة (mukabara)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazila rely solely on intuitive knowledge for generated acts.
Correction: The passage states that some (like Abu al-Husayn) claimed intuition, while the majority used inferential reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first aspect of the Mu'tazila's arguments for generated acts, according to al-Amidi?
A) The example of theoretical knowledge from reflection
B) The example of pushing a stone moving according to intention
C) The difference in capacities of strong and weak persons
D) The claim of intuitive knowledge by Abu al-Husayn

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila used both intuitive and inferential arguments to prove that generated acts are caused by human power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the stone example. Reflect on a recent action you took that had a chain of consequences. How do you view your role in those consequences? Are you fully responsible, or do you attribute them to God? Does this lesson's argument change how you think about your own agency?
?
The inferential argument points to the absurdity of a weak person moving a mountain if all acts were from God. In your own life, do you ever feel that your efforts are insufficient or that you rely too much on divine intervention? How does this argument push you to take more ownership of your actions?
?

Are you more convinced by direct experience or logical reasoning? How might this affect your faith in God's omnipotence and your personal accountability?

The First Argument: Command and Prohibition on Generated Acts
Presents the first inferential argument: command and prohibition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's first inferential argument for generated acts based on the occurrence of command and prohibition.

Core Idea
Commands and prohibitions on acts imply those acts are within created power.

The Mu'tazila argue that generated acts (al-mutawallidat) must be within human power because they are commanded and prohibited just like direct acts. This lesson unpacks the first argument, showing how commands to carry loads in war, build mosques, and seek knowledge demonstrate that such acts are mediated by created power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the first argument. Notice the examples given.
The argument is compact; let the examples illustrate the point.
2
Identify the key examples
Note the examples: carrying loads, building mosques, causing pain in jihad. They are all commanded or prohibited.
3
Understand the logic
If these acts were not within created power, commands would be absurd. The analogy with essences and colors reinforces this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاحتجاج فلهم فيه وجوه : الأول : ورود الأمر والنهي بها ) أي بالأفعال المتولدة ( كما ) وردا ( بالأفعال المباشرة وذلك كحمل الأثقال في الحروب والحدود وبناء المساجد والقناطر ) والمعارف ) النظرية كمعرفة الله تعالى وصفاته ومعرفة أحكام الشرع ) والإيلام) بالضرب والطعن والقتل في الجهاد مع الكفار فإنها كلها مأمور بها وجوباً أو ندباً وإيلام ما لا ينبغي إيلامه منهي عنه، فلولا أن هذه الأفعال متعلقة بالقدرة الحادثة لما التكليف بها والحث عليها كما لا يحسن التكليف بإيجاد الجواهر والألوان ولا شبهة في أنها ليست مباشرة بالقدرة فهي بواسطة ( الثاني : المدح والذم ) فإن العقلاء يستحسنون المدح والذم في أمثال هذه الأفعال ويحكمون باستحقاق الثواب والعقاب وذلك يدل على أنها من فعل العبد الثالث : نسبة الفعل إلى العبد دون الله ) كما حسن قوله : ( لا يقوم حجة عليهما وكذا لا يقوم حجة على ثمامة والنظام إذ ليس المتولدات عندهما من فعل العبد على ما في الأبكار حتى يلزم أحد المحذورين. قوله : ( فإن العقلاء يستحسنون المدح والذم إلخ) اعترض عليه بأن حسن المدح والذم لا يدل على استناد المتولد إلينا، وذلك لأن حسن الذم للمتولد حاصل وإن علمنا استناده إلى غيرنا فإنا ندم على إلقاء الصبي في النار إذا احترق بها مع أنا نعلم أن المحرق غير الملقي، وأجيب بأن الذم فيما ذكر على نفس الإلقاء المفضي إلى الإحراق عادة لا على الإحراق .
As for the inferential argument (al-ihtijaj), they have several aspects: The first: the occurrence of command and prohibition regarding them (i.e., generated acts) just as they occur regarding direct acts—such as carrying loads in wars, building mosques and bridges, theoretical knowledges (like knowledge of God and His attributes and knowledge of the rulings of the Law), and causing pain by beating, stabbing, and killing in jihad against the disbelievers. For all these are commanded (obligatorily or recommendably) and causing pain to what should not be pained is prohibited. Were these acts not related to created power, there would be no obligation (taklif) and encouragement regarding them, just as it is not proper to obligate the creation of essences (jawhar) and colors. And there is no doubt that these acts are not direct by power; so they are by mediation. The second: praise and blame (al-madh wa al-dhamm). For rational persons deem praise and blame proper regarding similar acts and judge that they deserve reward and punishment; this indicates that they are the act of the servant. The third: the attribution of the act to the servant rather than to God. As for the comment: 'His statement: (It does not stand as a proof against them) similarly it does not stand as a proof against Thumama and al-Nazzam, since generated acts are not the act of the servant according to them, as in al-Abkar, so that one of the two absurdities would follow.' His statement: 'For rational persons deem praise and blame proper...' it was objected: the goodness (husn) of praise and blame does not indicate that the generated act is attributable to us, because the goodness of blame for a generated act is attained even if we know it is attributable to another. For example, we blame someone for throwing a child into fire if it burns, even though we know that the burner is not the thrower. And it was answered: the blame in the mentioned case is for the throwing itself which usually leads to burning, not for the burning.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents the three inferential arguments (wujuh al-ihtijaj) of the Mu'tazila for attributing generated acts to the servant. The first argument is from command and prohibition: we find that acts like carrying loads in war, building mosques, seeking knowledge, and causing pain in jihad are commanded or prohibited. If these acts were not within the servant's power (qudra haditha), such commands would be invalid, just as it is absurd to command the creation of essences and colors. Since these acts are not direct, they must be by mediation (i.e., generated). The second argument is from praise and blame: rational people praise and blame for such acts and judge that they deserve reward and punishment, showing they are the servant's act. The third argument is from the attribution of the act to the servant rather than to God. The commentary then addresses two issues. First, a note: the objection about Dirar and Hafs does not apply to Thumama and al-Nazzam since they do not attribute generated acts to the servant. Second, an objection to the praise/blame argument: the goodness of blame does not necessarily prove the act is from us, as we blame someone for throwing a child in fire even though the burning is not from him. The answer clarifies: the blame is for the throwing (which causes burning by custom), not for the burning itself.

Key Terms
أمر (amr)
نهي (nahy)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Direct acts only
B) The occurrence of command and prohibition
C) The creation of essences
D) The impossibility of generated acts

Key Takeaway
Commands and prohibitions on generated acts prove they are within created power, as otherwise such commands would be invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson argues that commands and prohibitions on generated acts prove they are within your power. Is there a command of Allah that you have felt is beyond your ability? How does this argument challenge that feeling? What concrete step can you take this week to act as if it is within your power?
?
The passage mentions 'causing pain in jihad' as a commanded generated act. Think of a situation where you need to cause temporary discomfort for a righteous purpose (e.g., disciplining a child, giving difficult advice). Do you truly believe that act is within your created power? How does that belief change your willingness to do it?
?
The argument uses an analogy: commanding impossible acts is absurd. Examine your own life: have you ever dismissed a command of Allah as too difficult for someone—or for yourself? What does this lesson demand you reconsider? What will you commit to doing differently starting today?

The Second and Third Arguments: Praise/Blame and Attribution
Presents the second and third arguments, with an objection and response to the second.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's second and third inferential arguments for generated acts and the objection raised against the second argument.

Core Idea
Praise and blame, and attribution of acts, show generated acts belong to the servant.

The Mu'tazila present two more arguments: that rational people praise and blame for generated acts, implying they deserve reward or punishment, and that acts are attributed to the servant rather than God. This lesson also examines an objection to the praise/blame argument and its traditional answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
Read about praise and blame. Note that rational people consider these acts praiseworthy or blameworthy.
2
Read the third argument
The attribution of acts to the servant rather than God also supports human agency.
3
Analyse the objection and answer
Study the objection using the child-fire example and how the Mu'tazila answer it.
The answer distinguishes blame for the act from blame for the effect.

Mu'tazila Arguments: Direct Experience and Variation in Power
Mu'tazila argue that generated acts are caused by human power through two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite arguments from direct experience and variation in power for generated acts being caused by human power.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue generated acts are caused by human power through direct experience and variation in strength.

This passage presents two key Mu'tazilite proofs for the attribution of generated acts to human power. The first relies on direct experience, like a stone moving according to the pusher's intention. The second argues from the variation in power among humans. Both aim to show that such acts cannot be directly from God but must be mediated by human power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the two distinct arguments presented.
Focus on the flow from the necessity claim to the variation argument.
2
Identify the two arguments
Locate the first argument (al-ḍarūra) using the stone example, and the second argument (al-ikhtilāf) using the strong and weak arm example. Understand how each supports the claim that generated acts are from human power.
3
Reflect on the variation argument
Consider why the variation in generated acts according to human strength disproves direct divine creation. This is a key logical move in the Mu'tazilite case.
If God directly created all acts, a weak person could move a mountain, which is absurd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الثاني : في التوليد وفروعه. والمعتزلة القائلون بإسناد المتولدات إلى العباد ادعوا الضرورة تارة كأبي الحسين وأتباعه وجنحوا إلى الاستدلال أخرى كالجمهور منهم. أما الضرورة فقالوا : من رام دفع حجر في جهة اندفع إليها بحسب قصده وإرادته فيكون اندفاعه صادراً عن الدافع وفعلاً له، وليس هذا الاندفاع فعلاً له مباشراً بالاتفاق منا ومنكم فهو بواسطة ما باشره من الدفع ومتولد منه. وكذا الكلام في حصول العلم النظري من النظر وحصول أمثاله من أسبابها. واعلم أن الآمدي جعل اندفاع الحجر على حسب قصده وإرادته وجها أول من وجوه استدلالاتهم، وليس في كلامه ما يدل على أن أبا الحسين ادعى الضرورة هاهنا. ويؤيده اختلاف الأفعال التي سميت متولدة باختلاف القدر الثابتة للعباد فالأيد القوي يقوى على حمل ما لا يقوى على حمله الضعيف ولو كان الفعل المتولد واقعاً بقدرة الله لجاز تحرك الجبل باعتماد الضعيف النحيف وعدم تحرك الخردلة باعتماد الأيد القوي بأن يخلق الله الحركة في الجبل دون الخردلة وأنه مكابرة صرفة فاتضح أن المتولدات مستندة إلى القدرة الحادثة لا مباشرة بل بتوسط أفعال أخر. والآمدي جعل هذا التأييد وجهاً ثانياً من دلائلهم.
The Sixth Observatory - Second Intention: On Generation and Its Branches. The Mu'tazila who attribute generated acts to servants claimed necessity sometimes – like Abu al-Husayn and his followers – and inclined to argumentation at other times – like the majority of them. As for necessity, they said: whoever intends to push a stone in a direction, it moves according to his intention and will. So its movement is emanating from the pusher and is his act. And this movement is not his direct act by agreement (between us and you), so it is by means of what he directly performed of pushing and generated from it. Similarly for the acquisition of theoretical knowledge from reflection and similar things from their causes. Know that al-Amidi made the stone's movement according to intention and will a first aspect of their arguments, and there is nothing in his speech indicating that Abu al-Husayn claimed necessity here. And it is supported by the variation of acts called generated according to the variation of the power established for the servants. For the strong arm can carry what the weak cannot. If the generated act were by God's power, it would be possible for the mountain to move by the weak man's reliance and the mustard seed not to move by the strong man's reliance, if God created motion in the mountain but not the seed, and that is pure obstinacy. So it becomes clear that generated acts are attributed to the created power not directly but by means of other acts. And al-Amidi made this support a second aspect of their proofs.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite claim that generated acts (المتولدات) are caused by human power through two arguments: direct experience (الضرورة) and argument from variation (الاختلاف). The first argument uses the example of pushing a stone: the stone's movement is not a direct act of the pusher but is generated from the push, so it is attributed to his power. Similarly, theoretical knowledge arises from reflection. The second argument notes that generated acts vary with the strength of the servant (e.g., a strong arm can carry more than a weak one), which would be impossible if God directly created all acts, as then a weak person could move a mountain. Thus, generated acts must be caused by the created power via intermediate acts. Al-Amidi identifies these as the first and second aspects of their proofs.

Key Terms
توليد
متولدات
ضرورة
قدرة حادثة
مباشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila claim that humans directly create generated acts like God creates acts.
Correction: The Mu'tazila do not claim direct creation by humans; they claim generated acts occur via intermediary causes, not direct creation ex nihilo.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument (al-ḍarūra) the Mu'tazila give for generated acts being from human power?
A) The variation in power between strong and weak
B) The example of a stone moving according to the pusher's intention
C) The command and prohibition in scripture
D) The praise and blame by rational people

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila rely on direct experience (pushing a stone) and variation in human strength to argue that generated acts are caused by human power via intermediate generated acts, not directly by God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action that sets off a chain of events (like pushing a door open), do you instinctively take full credit for the entire outcome? How does the Mu'tazila's argument from direct experience challenge you to reconsider where your true causal power begins and ends?
?
The Mu'tazila use the fact that a strong person can lift more than a weak person as evidence that human power causes the lifting. In your own life, when you succeed or fail at a task, do you attribute it to your own ability alone, or do you recognize a deeper dependence on the Creator? Which tendency is more spiritually dangerous?
?
If you truly believed that every generated act—even the smallest movement—is directly created by God, how would your sense of urgency in prayer and reliance on God change? Would you still feel accountable for the consequences of your actions? Let this question unsettle your assumptions.

First Argument: Command and Prohibition
First argument based on command and prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila use the occurrence of command and prohibition to argue that generated acts are within human power.

Core Idea
Command implies capability; otherwise God commands the impossible.

The Mu'tazila argue that religious commands and prohibitions apply to generated acts, which must therefore be within human power. This lesson examines their first argument: the occurrence of command and prohibition regarding generated acts indicates they are subject to human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: premise, examples, conclusion.
The passage begins with the first aspect.
2
Identify the premise
The argument states that command and prohibition apply to generated acts as they do to direct acts. Understand that this implies these acts are within human power.
3
Analyze the examples
Consider the examples: carrying loads, building mosques, seeking knowledge, causing pain in jihad. How do they illustrate generated acts?
These are not direct but via means.
4
Compare with direct acts
The author notes that commanding the impossible (like creating substances) is not good. Contrast with generated acts to see the logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاحتجاج فلهم فيه وجوه : الأول : ورود الأمر والنهي بها أي بالأفعال المتولدة كما وردا بالأفعال المباشرة وذلك كحمل الأثقال في الحروب والحدود وبناء المساجد والقناطر والمعارف النظرية كمعرفة الله تعالى وصفاته ومعرفة أحكام الشرع والإيلام بالضرب والطعن والقتل في الجهاد مع الكفار فإنها كلها مأمور بها وجوباً أو ندباً وإيلام ما لا ينبغي إيلامه منهي عنه، فلولا أن هذه الأفعال متعلقة بالقدرة الحادثة لما التكليف بها والحث عليها كما لا يحسن التكليف بإيجاد الجواهر والألوان ولا شبهة في أنها ليست مباشرة بالقدرة فهي بواسطة. الثاني : المدح والذم فإن العقلاء يستحسنون المدح والذم في أمثال هذه الأفعال ويحكمون باستحقاق الثواب والعقاب وذلك يدل على أنها من فعل العبد. الثالث : نسبة الفعل إلى العبد دون الله كما في قولهم : حمل فلان الثقيل وآلم زيداً بالضرب، وليس هذا من قبيل المجاز عندهم بل من الإسناد الحقيقي فدل على أن الفعل منه. اعترض عليه بأن حسن المدح والذم لا يدل على استناد المتولد إلينا، وذلك لأن حسن الذم للمتولد حاصل وإن علمنا استناده إلى غيرنا فإنا ندم على إلقاء الصبي في النار إذا احترق بها مع أنا نعلم أن المحرق غير الملقي، وأجيب بأن الذم فيما ذكر على نفس الإلقاء المفضي إلى الإحراق عادة لا على الإحراق.
As for argumentation, they have aspects: First: the occurrence of command and prohibition regarding them (i.e., generated acts) as they occur regarding direct acts, such as carrying heavy loads in wars and punishments, building mosques and bridges, theoretical knowledge such as knowledge of God Most High and His attributes and knowledge of rulings of the law, and causing pain by hitting, stabbing, and killing in jihad against disbelievers – all of these are commanded (obligatorily or recommendably) and causing pain to what should not be pained is prohibited. Were it not that these acts are related to the created power, there would be no obligation or encouragement for them, just as it is not good to oblige the creation of substances and colors; and there is no doubt that they are not direct by power, so they are by means. Second: Praise and blame – for rational people deem praise and blame good in such acts and judge deserving of reward and punishment, and that indicates they are from the servant's act. Third: Attribution of the act to the servant rather than God, as in their saying: 'So-and-so carried the heavy load' and 'Zayd caused pain by hitting'; this is not metaphorical for them but real attribution, so it indicates the act is from him. An objection was raised: that the goodness of praise and blame does not indicate the generated act is based on us, because the goodness of blame for a generated act occurs even if we know it is based on someone else – for we blame someone for throwing a child into fire if he burns, even though we know the burner is not the thrower. And it was answered: blame in the mentioned case is for the throwing itself which leads to burning by custom, not for the burning.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage lists three Mu'tazilite arguments for generated acts being attributable to human power. First, the argument from command and prohibition: religious obligations (like carrying loads in war, building mosques, seeking knowledge, causing pain in jihad) apply to generated acts, which must therefore be within human power; otherwise, commanding them would be like commanding the impossible (e.g., creating substances). Second, the argument from praise and blame: rational people praise or blame others for such acts, indicating they are the agent's own deeds. Third, the argument from linguistic attribution: phrases like 'he carried' or 'he hurt' are taken literally, not metaphorically, showing the act is truly from the servant. The passage then includes an objection against the second argument: blame can apply even when the agent is not the direct cause (e.g., throwing a child into fire leads to burning, but the burner is the fire). The answer distinguishes: blame is for the throw, which customarily leads to burning, not for the burning itself. Thus, the objection is countered.

Key Terms
أمر
نهي
متولدة
مباشرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Generated acts are not truly within human power because they involve external means.
Correction: The Mu'tazila argue that command and prohibition imply capability; the means are part of that capability.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, why does the occurrence of command and prohibition indicate generated acts are within human power?
A) Because God only commands direct acts
B) Because commanding the impossible is not good
C) Because all acts are directly from God
D) Because reward and punishment depend on knowledge

Key Takeaway
The first Mu'tazilite argument holds that divine command and prohibition regarding generated acts prove they are within human power, since commanding the impossible is rationally impermissible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that every commanded act (like seeking knowledge or jihad) is within your power because God commands it, what one specific act have you been avoiding that you will commit to doing this week?
?
The Mu'tazilite argument uses the fact that God prohibits causing unjust pain as proof that we have the power to avoid it. In your own life, where have you excused yourself from avoiding a sin by claiming you couldn't help it? How does this argument challenge that excuse?
?
Consider one area where you feel powerless to obey a command of Allah. Does this lesson convince you otherwise? What practical step will you take to act on that conviction starting today?

Second Argument: Praise and Blame
Second argument based on praise and blame

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila use the universal practice of praise and blame to argue that generated acts are from the servant's own doing.

Core Idea
Praise and blame only apply to acts within the agent's control.

The second argument appeals to a universal human practice: we praise and blame people for generated acts like carrying loads or causing pain. This lesson explores why the Mu'tazila see this as evidence that such acts are truly the servant's own deeds.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Identify the claim: rational people praise/blame for generated acts.
This is the second aspect listed.
2
Understand the reasoning
Why does praise and blame indicate the act is from the servant? The reasoning is that we only praise or blame for acts we consider the person's own doing.
3
Connect to reward and punishment
The passage mentions deserving reward and punishment. How does that strengthen the argument?

Source Text

As for argumentation, they have aspects: First: the occurrence of command and prohibition regarding them (i.e., generated acts) as they occur regarding direct acts, such as carrying heavy loads in wars and punishments, building mosques and bridges, theoretical knowledge such as knowledge of God Most High and His attributes and knowledge of rulings of the law, and causing pain by hitting, stabbing, and killing in jihad against disbelievers – all of these are commanded (obligatorily or recommendably) and causing pain to what should not be pained is prohibited. Were it not that these acts are related to the created power, there would be no obligation or encouragement for them, just as it is not good to oblige the creation of substances and colors; and there is no doubt that they are not direct by power, so they are by means. Second: Praise and blame – for rational people deem praise and blame good in such acts and judge deserving of reward and punishment, and that indicates they are from the servant's act. Third: Attribution of the act to the servant rather than God, as in their saying: 'So-and-so carried the heavy load' and 'Zayd caused pain by hitting'; this is not metaphorical for them but real attribution, so it indicates the act is from him. An objection was raised: that the goodness of praise and blame does not indicate the generated act is based on us, because the goodness of blame for a generated act occurs even if we know it is based on someone else – for we blame someone for throwing a child into fire if he burns, even though we know the burner is not the thrower. And it was answered: blame in the mentioned case is for the throwing itself which leads to burning by custom, not for the burning.

Commentary
This passage lists three Mu'tazilite arguments for generated acts being attributable to human power. First, the argument from command and prohibition: religious obligations (like carrying loads in war, building mosques, seeking knowledge, causing pain in jihad) apply to generated acts, which must therefore be within human power; otherwise, commanding them would be like commanding the impossible (e.g., creating substances). How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
